# What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

_195 messages · 33 participants · 2003-05 → 2003-12_

**Topics:** [`COBOL's future, legacy, and obsolescence`](../topics.md#future) · [`Help requests and how-to`](../topics.md#help)

---

### What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-23T16:15:16-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
 J4 and WG4 posed the question "What <is> your vision for the COBOL language
5 years from now?"

Although, I haven't heard it officially yet, I believe that I have heard it
from sufficiently "reliable sources" that CobolExpo 2003 (for June in Las
Vegas) is now canceled.  I also BELIEVE that as of this moment, the "forum
on the future of COBOL" is still planned to take place that week in Las
Vegas.  Anyone already planning on attending the forum (originally in
addition to the CobolExpo) should verify exactly what the plans are for it.

HOWEVER, the major reason that I am posting this note to a few groups and
lists is that I really think that "we all" need to step up to the actual
answer to the question of where COBOL will be 5 years from now - and the
answer is close to (but not quite) irrelevant.

I *strongly* believe that there will still be COBOL compilers (even some
with new releases, new features, and new interfaces) - not only 5 years from
now but even 10 years from now.

I even believe (from some documents submitted to J4) that there MAY be one
or two compilers that conform (at least mostly) to the 2002 ISO COBOL
Standard.

I suspect that there will even be new and "upgraded" COBOL applications that
use NEW features introduced in the 2002 COBOL Standard - and some of the
TR's (Technical Reports) currently being worked on by J4 and WG4.

    ON THE OTHER HAND,

The following are some of my "crystal ball" visions (whether "fears" or
simply "forebodings")

1) I expect that MOST COBOL compilers will use the 2002 ISO Standard (and
the TR's - if approved) as a "Chinese menu" and will "pick and choose" which
of the (required - not just processor dependent) features they ever
implement.

2) COBOL will NEVER be a *major* player in the "user-side" of the web or
server application environment. (Which is NOT to say that there won't be
some new - as well as continued - applications that do this.)

3) New "businesses" doing data processing or existing businesses creating
NEW applications will *only* select COBOL as their "programming language of
choice" in infrequent and unusual circumstances.

4) Sites with existing COBOL applications will increase their
"consideration" of language conversions away from COBOL when they do "major
maintenance" or new functionality to those programs.

5) Membership in J4 and WG4 will continue to "decrease" with NO new COBOL
vendors joining J4; no new countries joining WG4; and those doing the "real
work" of the committees continuing to be limited to a VERY SMALL handful of
individuals (not vendors or user companies).

6) There never will be robust  "certification tests" for the 2002 ISO
Standard - and the US government (along with European and Asian governmental
bodies) will NEVER require "2002 conforming" compilers.  (And possibly they
will even - within the next 10 years - drop COBOL as a "authorized"
programming language for new projects - in cases where they have a list of
such authorized programming "languages" or "tools").

        ***

Bottom-Line:
  I will continue to "post" to newsgroups and others the information of what
is happening with "development" and "maintenance" of the COBOL language by
various Standards groups.  I think it is important that all existing COBOL
programmers "keep abreast" of what is or might be happening or going to
happen.

OTOH,  I can't see - unless something DRAMATICALLY changes - that COBOL will
be seen as a GROWING programming environment.  Certainly the 30-year old
question of "Is COBOL dead?" still deserves an answer of NO.  On the other
hand, the question of "Is COBOL a dynamic/growing programming environment?"
seems to warrant an equally strong "NO" as an answer.

OBVIOUSLY, this post reflects PERSONAL OPINION only.  I wouldn't mind being
proven wrong - but I won't hold my breath either.
```

#### ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca>
- **Date:** 2003-05-23T23:27:09+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ECEAAA3.B5AE0D4A@shaw.ca>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`

```


"William M. Klein" wrote:

>  J4 and WG4 posed the question "What <is> your vision for the COBOL language
> 5 years from now?"
>

<snip>

Oh my gawd ! (Enter.... stage left,  Pete Dashwood arms flailing ..... <G>)

Even the J4/WG4 question is loaded. Bearing in mind, (J4 at least), being
comprised of 6 compiler vendors, out of a current total of 9 - they didn't even
really need to ask the question did they ? They must have the pulse of their
existing developer database, such as how many installations are just plodding
along with old compilers rather than playing catch-up, or how many of their
developers have pleaded for new features to compete against the language du
jour.

So perhaps we should be internalizing - my own thoughts :-

(1) Mainframers nearing retirement - do they care, or is it a case of '"I'm gong
to sweat it out with COBOL, unchanged, until I go out of the door with my
pension"..

(2) "Pooh, pooh, pooh. We've heard this one before. COBOL will still be around
even when Noah II builds his ark".

(3) I like what I've got - why all this OO COBOL nonsense ? Besides, I'll stick
with COBOL for the grunt work and use other languages and tools for GUI-ing the
Web etc., coupled with SQL and a DB engine.

(4) People keep adamantly denying it - but COBOL education shows a dramatic
decline. (I recall Thane writing, some 3 or 4 (?) Java-types had switched to
COBOL down there in Texas. That's not good enough. Had it been, 'some 500
Java-types had switched.....'.).

(5) No generalizations as answers please. The VB, C and Java crowd. Accepting
that the training 'academies' made a conscious switch to teaching new languages
and often dropping COBOL completely from the syllabus, put your finger on where
you think their developers  found COBOL to be weak. What is it that the other
languages offer that COBOL just misses out on, (at least in their perception).

(6) Signs that COBOL is in decline - Some years back, I had myself geared up
thinking of attending a Micro Focus get together. Too late. It was cancelled and
one held since. If my memory is correct the cbobolreport first Expo was
cancelled for lack of interest. So they had Expo 2002 - but no idea of
attendance. COBOL 2003 - now a goner (?) - it is not a paying proposition.

(7) (a) Comments like, we will stick with current compiler, can't cost justify
buying, say, IBM Enterprise.

(7) (b) "Yer gets what yer pays for.....". Tech support fees and upgrades to
compilers - big obstacle in the PC world.  (I haven't even mentioned the R..
T... Fees ).

(8) (a) J4's role. I don't want to belabour this one. Some (all ?) compiler
vendors introduced extensions beyond COBOL '85 to be sanctioned originally in a
targeted COBOL '97 which became COBOL 2002 (that's December 2002), so we are
talking 2003 for implementation in new compilers.

(8) (b) My particular interest - OO COBOL was kick-started in '92/93,  now
formally sanctioned in COBOL 2002 - but as at the date or writing, Fujitsu,
Hitachi and Micro Focus are still AHEAD of the COBOL 2002 Standard - with
interim extensions for destroying objects (garbage collection) and more
importantly a set of  'Standard Classes' which include the very elemental Base
Class and lists (collections/dictionaries).

(8) (c) Here's what I really don't understand. Visualize yourself as the #2 or
#3 head honcho at say, Fujitsu or Micro Focus. You get sit reps from your J4 man
as to what is going on. Considering what has happened, I could see myself in
such a position, asking, "What the F..... are you people doing at these
meetings. We've had  "xxxxx"  as a f........ extension since ...(pick a date).
What the F.... is going on ! We have an investment in this people. Money ".

I am *NOT* knocking the J4 participants and am fully aware, as Bill has
observed, that the real donkey work is done by a dedicated minority. BUT - as an
executive of a compiler vendor, I would still find myself asking the above
questions - and verbalized, there wouldn't be any dots after the "F's".

(9) In all seriousness - Is it time to call it quits on a future COBOL Standard
? With forty years behind us the syntactical pattern of COBOL is  well
established. Is it time for the compiler vendors to opt out, do their own thing,
and introduce new features (extensions), as they see fit, to compete against the
other languages ?

Jimmy, Calgary AB.
```

#### ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-05-23T23:37:01+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
Why has this happened? Where did we fail?

Is the language inherently flawed? CS types have been saying that for 30 years
without much effect. 

Because the user community feels gouged by Y2K contractors? They have been, and
continue to be, gouged by contracting companies proffering Indian H1B visa
programmers who are paid 20% of the bill rate. Gouging didn't start nor end with
Y2K projects, nor is it specific to COBOL.  

Because standards committees took 17 years to produce the next 'release'?
That's almost the lifetime of the PC and, to my thinking, twice the lifetime of
the 'modern PC', which began when Windows 95 was released. One would reasonably
expect a vibrant, growing language to be enhanced every two to four years.
Seventeen years is out of the ballpark. It creates a Public Image problem. 

Because COBOL is not perceived as modern? If so, why doesn't ANSI C a'la 1980
suffer the same opprobium? It is still widely used. OO COBOL is modern,
especially in a .NET environment, yet nobody seems to notice. 

I've got it -- because decision-making managers' technical skills are usually
ten years old. They are assessing COBOL as it was in 1995 rather than today. If
it had been vibrant and forward-looking then, they would support it now. If it
had been 'on the edge of the horizon' pushing GUI -- a hot topic in 1995 -- they
would regard it as a vibrant language today. Reality doesn't count here, only
ten year-old perceptions. 

Well, we failed miserably. In 1995 we *could* have been supporting calls to MFC
to paint GUI screens. Instead we stared at our navels and legacy code, thinking
this too shall pass. Now we're paying the price.

>5 years from now?"
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>

```
Thane Hubbell wrote:

>> Well, we failed miserably. In 1995 we *could* have been supporting calls
>> to MFC to paint GUI screens. Instead we stared at our navels and legacy
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> selling SP2 - specifically for GUI and COBOL.

I have also used Hi-Screen-Pro II for Cobol applications, it was available 
in 1992 (don't know when 'I' was) and could be used by several languages 
including MF, MS, RM and Realia Cobol, Foxpro, Pascal, C, well anything 
really.

From 1991, starting with MF versionm 2.4, I (and I do mean I working alone 
most of the time) developed a fully graphical container loading system 
doing drag and drop of containers from wharf stacks into graphical ship 
bays with windows for stability, torsion, bending curves, bonjean views of 
the hull, even a view from the helm of the containers showing the horizon 
(correctly set for the calculated vessel trim) to confirm the appropriate 
visability requirements.  The 'helm' position was variable so by changing 
this to some large numbers the view could be from half a mile behind the 
ship at a couple of hundred feet and some distance to the side.  The 
program would then redraw it and change the view point to show a 'fly by'.

All done in Cobol directly to Windows API on Windows 3.0 originally, 
updated over the years to 3.11 and Win32.

We don't need no steenking MFC.

RW may have been staring at his navel, I certainly wasn't, nor was anyone 
else who wanted to.
```

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote in message
news:bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net...
> J4 and WG4 posed the question "What <is> your vision for the COBOL

>

Nobody asked for my opinion. (Probably because they know it wouldn't be what
they want to hear <G>)

I have some very definite views on the "future of COBOL", not just over five
years but for 10 and 15 years as well.

I also have some pretty definite views on how it "fell from Grace" and what
could have been done about it. (Not to "whip cats", just as interesting
speculation with the possibility of profiting from past mistakes.)

And it is NOT "irrelevant". The news is NOT all bad. The use of COBOL will
change and there will be some major adjustments at Corporate level. I expect
to be writing COBOL (and other languages) as long as I can see and type. (Of
course, whether I'll be making any money out of it is another point
entirely...<G>)

> Although, I haven't heard it officially yet, I believe that I have heard
it
> from sufficiently "reliable sources" that CobolExpo 2003 (for June in Las
> Vegas) is now canceled.  I also BELIEVE that as of this moment, the "forum
> on the future of COBOL" is still planned to take place that week in Las
> Vegas.  Anyone already planning on attending the forum (originally in
> addition to the CobolExpo) should verify exactly what the plans are for
it.
>

Presumably, if the response has been so weak as to cause cancellation of the
Expo, it is unlikely that many will attend the forum. Asking people to stump
up several hundred dollars (plus expenses, plus lost earnings if you are a
freelance) is a big ask in the current economic conditions. The result is
that the main interest in the Expo will be from vendors and sponsors(who can
afford to attend), but have a position to promote. You can bet that
corporate sponsored presentations on the "Future of COBOL" will be little
more than thinly veiled sales spiel based on the premise that "the future of
COBOL is rosy if you buy our product...".

> HOWEVER, the major reason that I am posting this note to a few groups and
> lists is that I really think that "we all" need to step up to the actual
> answer to the question of where COBOL will be 5 years from now - and the
> answer is close to (but not quite) irrelevant.
>

Well, that is one answer, Bill. However, it is NOT the only one.

> I *strongly* believe that there will still be COBOL compilers (even some
> with new releases, new features, and new interfaces) - not only 5 years
from
> now but even 10 years from now.
>

Possibly hand carved by devotees of the Language as a cottage industry? (I
predicted some years back that a number of the current vendors will "leave
the field" by 2015. IBM are hanging in there only because they HAVE to and
not because they WANT to. As soon as their Java and Websphere offerings have
an established market place, I predict their focus will move away from
COBOL.)


> I even believe (from some documents submitted to J4) that there MAY be one
> or two compilers that conform (at least mostly) to the 2002 ISO COBOL
> Standard.
>
> I suspect that there will even be new and "upgraded" COBOL applications
that
> use NEW features introduced in the 2002 COBOL Standard - and some of the
> TR's (Technical Reports) currently being worked on by J4 and WG4.
>

So what? Does anybody care? The organizations administering the COBOL
standard ARE irrelevant, now. They have earned this position in the same way
that everybody either earns or loses respect: by their actions and their
performance.

But that doesn't make COBOL irrelevant. The language is bigger than the
organisations that tried (successfully) to hijack it from the public domain
and put their own copyright on it.

>     ON THE OTHER HAND,
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> the TR's - if approved) as a "Chinese menu" and will "pick and choose"
which
> of the (required - not just processor dependent) features they ever
> implement.
>

That's no bad thing. At least some vendors will be continung to produce a
COBOL product. I'd rather see vendors (who are MUCH more in touch with
market demand) decide what should be in the Language, than a bunch of
boondoggling wannabes who have a meeting every 10 minutes in oprder to
pretend they're actually achieving something.


> 2) COBOL will NEVER be a *major* player in the "user-side" of the web or
> server application environment. (Which is NOT to say that there won't be
> some new - as well as continued - applications that do this.)
>

It is at my place...<G>.

Again, there are a number of things COBOL is extremely useful for. Some of
these things relate to the future of computing in general and the Web in
particular. There is far too much to cover here and until somebody asks me,
I won't be saying anything on the subject. (Some of it is unpalatable to
certain people and I don't want to be accused of simply stirring, as has
happened in the past...<G>. Overall though, my view on the future of COBOL
is positive. Well, at least as positive as any other language...)

> 3) New "businesses" doing data processing or existing businesses creating
> NEW applications will *only* select COBOL as their "programming language
of
> choice" in infrequent and unusual circumstances.

They won't use ANY programming language. The days of "computer programming"
for businesses are almost over. It was a phenomenon of the latter half of
the 20th Century, just like cottage lace making in the early 19th century. I
have a lot to say about this...<G>

>
> 4) Sites with existing COBOL applications will increase their
> "consideration" of language conversions away from COBOL when they do
"major
> maintenance" or new functionality to those programs.
>

Only in the short to medium term. There are events in the pipeline that will
change everything very drastically. It is very hard for people raised on
COBOL and the Waterfall to get their heads around it. Basically, the whole
way in which we implement computer applications into business (and the home,
for that matter) will undergo a sea change.


> 5) Membership in J4 and WG4 will continue to "decrease" with NO new COBOL
> vendors joining J4; no new countries joining WG4;

God, I hope so! The sooner these people get off COBOL's back, the better for
everybody.

>and those doing the "real
> work" of the committees continuing to be limited to a VERY SMALL handful
of
> individuals (not vendors or user companies).

Yes. I don't want to throw the baby out with the bath water... There are
some very dedicated and hard-working people who have contributed to these
committees (your own contribution is not insignificant, Bill).
Unfortunately, due to a number of reasons which I won't go into here, the
achievements of this group were abysmal. My criticism is not directed at
individuals; it is directed at the whole edifice and its lack of
answerability, management, and, consequently, achievement.


>
> 6) There never will be robust  "certification tests" for the 2002 ISO
> Standard - and the US government (along with European and Asian
governmental
> bodies) will NEVER require "2002 conforming" compilers.  (And possibly
they
> will even - within the next 10 years - drop COBOL as a "authorized"
> programming language for new projects - in cases where they have a list of
> such authorized programming "languages" or "tools").
>

Who cares? Why does it matter? (I reckon it doesn't...)

>         ***
>
> Bottom-Line:
>   I will continue to "post" to newsgroups and others the information of
what
> is happening with "development" and "maintenance" of the COBOL language by
> various Standards groups.  I think it is important that all existing COBOL
> programmers "keep abreast" of what is or might be happening or going to
> happen.
>

Why is it "important" Bill? The average COBOL grunt working in the Fortress
has no realistic say or control over what the standard does, and
furthermore, it isn't high on his/her list of priorities. When s(he) has
problems with syntax, it isn't the Standard manual s(he) reaches for, it is
the vendor's implementation. The people on the shop floor, for the most
part, simply don't care, and there is no reason why they should. The only
people we see jumping up and down about Standards are people who are on the
committee or have a vested interest. (As it costs a considerable amount to
join the club, you can understand the Members being sensitive about getting
value...)

> OTOH,  I can't see - unless something DRAMATICALLY changes - that COBOL
will
> be seen as a GROWING programming environment.  Certainly the 30-year old
> question of "Is COBOL dead?" still deserves an answer of NO.  On the other
> hand, the question of "Is COBOL a dynamic/growing programming
environment?"
> seems to warrant an equally strong "NO" as an answer.
>

Yes, that is probably fair. But there are other factors that need to be
considered here. As I hinted above, the whole way in which we do things is
undergoing change. It isn't just about COBOL, although, sadly,  COBOL is one
of the least equipped to deal with it. (This is no fault of the vendors...)


> OBVIOUSLY, this post reflects PERSONAL OPINION only.  I wouldn't mind
being
> proven wrong - but I won't hold my breath either.
>

Amen to that <G>!

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** Peter Lacey <lacey@mb.sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2003-05-24T16:25:32-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ECFE34C.898BF0F8@mb.sympatico.ca>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <3eceb533_3@corp-news.newsgroups.com> <ban0i1$fdh$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3ecf74d9_2@corp-news.newsgroups.com>`

```
Let me offer an analogy.

Most of you more experienced types will have heard of PRIME computers, and
perhaps the associated INFOBASIC language.  Primemnever gained more than a tiny
percentage of the market and has been out of business for years.  The machines
were very solid and the O/S bulletproof (as long as the machine wasn't actually
malfunctioning); the language, although very labour-intensive and having some
very unusual ideas, allowed for a great deal of productivity; a 32-meg 5-mips
machine with 4-5 g of disk storage could support 60 users + batch processing
with 1-2 second response time.  However, they never secured more than a niche.
I'm  working with a company that is still using one!  The O/S & compiler are
dated 1992!  There is a company in Toronto that still finds it worthwhile to
offer hardware & software support for the Prime machines.  There is a conversion
method available, to IBM-blessed UNIVERSE/UNIDATA; quite a number of companies
that I know of went this route and are running their 1992-vintage applications
in what amounts to Infobasic to this day.

The point is that while COBOL may have been pronounced old, decrepit, or even
dead - given the sheer mass of current application written in Cobol it would
suprise me if there WEREN'T many of them still going 30 years from now!

Peter the Iconoclast of Pontefract
```

```
Richard Plinston wrote:

> Peter Lacey wrote:
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pick and Universe is a version of Pick (or a look-alike) running on Unix.

Both ways, Prime & Pr1me although the latter seems to me to be "cute".

I have a document somewhere on the origins of the language, but not at hand.
Universe is a  language that was designed to encompass as much as possible of
ALL the variants, such as Pick & Infobasic, so that users wouldn't be stuck
with the demise of the various vendors.  VMARK started, Ardent bought them,
Informix bought them, and now IBM has business partners that support it.

> > Peter the Iconoclast of Pontefract
>
> Are you really in Pontefract amongst the fields of licorice ?  It's just
> that my daughter will be there next week.

No, it's just that the deLacy's held most of Worcestershire and a good part
of England a thousand years ago (we've fallen on hard times since) and I've
always fancied the name.  (We moved to Canada in 1957).  I was especially
tickled to hear a reference to Pontefract on an episode of "The Upchat Line".

Peter
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-05-24T23:24:56+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ecff487.149356514@news.optonline.net>`

```
Peter Lacey <lacey@mb.sympatico.ca> wrote:

> a 32-meg 5-mips
>machine with 4-5 g of disk storage could support 60 users + batch processing
>with 1-2 second response time.  

I got the same performance from an 8-meg, 1-mips, 1 g disk supporting 100 users
+ batch, on an IBM 4361. The difference was my machine was executing COBOL
compiled to *machine language*; the PRIME was running a p-code interpreter. 

Over and over .. a hundred times over .. we've seen the speed ratio between
native code and p-code come in between 5:1 and 10:1. It is a cosmic constant. 

>The point is that while COBOL may have been pronounced old, decrepit, or even
>dead - given the sheer mass of current application written in Cobol it would
>suprise me if there WEREN'T many of them still going 30 years from now!

Have you ever noticed that people using obsolete technology not only don't brag
about it, they don't even like to talk about it? So too, compilers using
interpreted p-code (e.g. AccuCobol) try to hide that fact? 

Are we COBOL programmers destined to become the shame of the industry, hidden in
the basement to avoid embarrassing 'forward looking' management?

It seems that way. Now that we have global networking, the 'basement' can be in
Bangalore, India, and Montgomery, Alabama. That's ok with me. I know first-hand
that Montgomery is a better place than NYC, Washington DC and Silicon Valley.
I've never been to India but surmise the people of Bangalore probably also enjoy
good manners and little traffic congestion.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-05-24T22:17:10+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ecf9ad2.126388208@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <3eceb533_3@corp-news.newsgroups.com>`

```
"Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>They won't use ANY programming language. The days of "computer programming"
>for businesses are almost over. It was a phenomenon of the latter half of
>the 20th Century, just like cottage lace making in the early 19th century. I
>have a lot to say about this...<G>

Business has succeeded by turning high-risk craft skills into low-risk routine
work which can be performed on an assembly line or machine. 

In every decade, a panacea has claimed it can do the same with programming. In
the 60s, it was decision tables. In the 70s, proprietary code generators. In the
80s, CASE tools. In the 90s, OO and methodologies such as CMM and ISO9000. 

During that time, the number of full-time, paid programmers (in the US) has gone
from 50,000 to 5,000,000. 

Now we're told that Components will make our skills obsolete. Having heard the
same song for forty years, we're understandably skeptical.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-05-25T14:30:23+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ed027f4_2@corp-news.newsgroups.com>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <3eceb533_3@corp-news.newsgroups.com> <3ecf9ad2.126388208@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3ecf9ad2.126388208@news.optonline.net...
> "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
> >They won't use ANY programming language. The days of "computer
programming"
> >for businesses are almost over. It was a phenomenon of the latter half of
> >the 20th Century, just like cottage lace making in the early 19th
century. I
> >have a lot to say about this...<G>
>
> Business has succeeded by turning high-risk craft skills into low-risk
routine
> work which can be performed on an assembly line or machine.
>

Yes, it has. And sometimes when it was said to be impossible. (The history
of the Nottingham Lace industry is an excellent example of this. The
perceived wisdom was that no machine could ever be built that could do such
intricate work. There were many failed attempts and destroyed lives and
fortunes. It took 40 years, but it was achieved.)

> In every decade, a panacea has claimed it can do the same with
programming. In
> the 60s, it was decision tables. In the 70s, proprietary code generators.
In the
> 80s, CASE tools. In the 90s, OO and methodologies such as CMM and ISO9000.
>

Yes, I remember "The Last One". Closely followed by "The Next One"...<G>.

 However, the fact that no-one has succeeded so far, doesn't mean that no
one will EVER succeed.

Besides, the whole way in which applications are developed is starting to
change and the way in which Corporations perceive IT is also changing. From
being a "mysterious arcane Black Art" that was only knowable by its
practitioners, the level of computer literacy is going up exponentially. As
people become more familiar and comfortable with computers they are
demanding more from them. It is not acceptable to have to wait on the whim
of some technocrats before applications can be delivered.

Computer Science and Software developers are racing to meet the demand for
simple, user friendly interfaces, that can be iterated towards a solution
with no deep level of technical knowledge required. In some areas we are
already seeing end users (rather than programmers, as was previously the
case) interacting with software to achieve the results THEY want, (not the
results the IT department THINK they want or should have). It has to be
coupled with a completely different, much more RAD approach, but I have seen
places where it is being done.

(I have been instrumental in introducing it in some places, and it is very
satisfying to see the way Users react to being empowered to build their own
systems. It is not very popular with the IT department <surprise!
surprise!...> and most of the battles I've had have been with IT management
rather than the user management. To be fair, some IT people also realise
that, while it may mean a change of role for them, it is much closer to
delivering the true goals of IT. (Support of the Business...in case anyone's
forgotten <G>)).

The Waterfall is being kept alive by IT people who see it as the last means
of retaining control. It is doomed and when it falls so will the Fortress...
I take no particular pleasure in this; I manned the Fortress for a large
part of my working life, but the time has come to declare the War over and
abandon it.

> During that time, the number of full-time, paid programmers (in the US)
has gone
> from 50,000 to 5,000,000.

Yep, there's a lot of guys writing VB and Java...<G>

>
> Now we're told that Components will make our skills obsolete. Having heard
the
> same song for forty years, we're understandably skeptical.
>

You haven't heard the same song for forty years. The technology hasn't been
around for forty years. You can assume that there was "equivalent"
technology forty years ago, but there wasn't. There were "similar concepts"
from which the latest stuff evolved. Anyway, I am not arguing that Component
technology will make programming skills obsolete.

Read this carefully; when I use words I try to say EXACTLY what I mean...

"Component technology is just ONE factor (albeit, a key one) that will lead
to the removal of in-house computer programming for commercial
installations. There are many other factors and it is the COMBINATION of ALL
these things that will render "computer programming" as we understand it,
redundant."

That is a long way from telling you that Components will make your skills
obsolete. Business decisions are going to do that.

It isn't just about technology. Businesses are starting to question the high
costs of in-house IT. The result is a move towards packages like Siebel and
SAP, coupled with outsourcing and buying temporary expertise for the period
it is required (rather than putting these specialists on the corporate
pension fund...)
(Note that both Siebel and SAP support component technology so that their
functionality can be more easily modified to suit individual requirements,
WITHOUT non-standard re-configuration of the basic package...Siebel Version
7 supports the full 3 tier model; previous versions were 2 tier and thus
limited in what non-standard functions you could include.)

It is already happening, though perhaps not in the United States, where they
seem more content to maintain the status quo, but simply push all the grunt
work to India, as the bottom line is all that  matters.
(I'm sure there are companies in the US who are embracing approaches that
utilise the new technology, but as I don't work there, I can't speak from
first hand experience. I wouldn't mind betting that readers of this
newsgroup have seen the Fortress being replaced on at least some US sites.)

Yes, there have been many Daniel's coming along blowing their horns, and
leading everybody off into the Wilderness. Until they find their way back to
COBOL. Been there, seen it. And COBOL has always been capable of doing what
was required. (Still is, but the tragedy is that many (most?) COBOL users
won't push the envelope or even begin to think that there might be better
ways of doing things (even with COBOL)).

This time the ground rules are different. COBOL is no longer the only game
in town.

And "computer programming" is no longer the only way to deliver applications
to users.

Deal with it.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-05-26T17:01:00+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ed19ce9_2@corp-news.newsgroups.com>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <3eceb533_3@corp-news.newsgroups.com> <3ecf9ad2.126388208@news.optonline.net> <3ed027f4_2@corp-news.newsgroups.com> <3ed16a8d.245120561@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3ed16a8d.245120561@news.optonline.net...
> "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> >"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
>
> Every formal methodology that I've seen makes the user a participant
throughout
> the development process.
>

Then you need to get out more...<G>

The formal Waterfall methodology (which is in place, carved in stone, on the
majority of Fortress sites), requires sign off of each phase, and positively
discourages user involvement once requirements gathering is complete.

Once it is signed off the users are NOT involved. In fact, further
involvement by them is discouraged by IT until the phase is complete and
they have something to deliver. The idea is that users wanting continual
changes will simply make the project late and the requests for change are
considered an irritation and distraction to the IT department, so they are
referred to "Change Control" via the Project Office (which is very often run
and adminstered by IT), as a means of discouraging such requests. It is
hardly any wonder that the final product is not what is needed.(Instead, it
is "something like" what was needed six months ago...)

RAD, on the other hand, encourages change as a means of evolving the system.
New requirements and enhancements are welcomed and prioritised jointly with
the Business, on the understanding that development is timeboxed and when
the time is up, that's it.

> Users have been developing their own systems for years, using tools like
Access.
> Most of those systems are amateurish, lacking basic controls and edit
checks.
>

Yet they get better every year and users love them.

What makes you think that IT people have a monopoly on systems development
ability? History? Because "we're trained and they're not"?

It may have escaped your notice, but the world has changed. The last place I
worked (up until 2 months ago, so it is recent) there were more people with
computer science degrees in the User area than there were in the IT
department. Some of their "amateurish, control-and-edit lacking" systems
were better than the stuff they got from IT.

On the projects where we got to work together with them we were able to
achieve some fantastic results, but I had the Devil's own job getting the IT
Management to even run pilots that did not involve the Waterfall. ("It's a
standard, we can't change it.") OK, don't change it, just run a pilot that
is exempt from it and see what happens. Use a system that isn't critical...
no risk, what can you lose...?

After the first one we had people in the business and IT queueing up to work
on the next one.

(And a covert counter attack from IT Middle Management, where they prevented
access to Web Sites that discussed RAD, and destroyed copies of documents I
had circulated on the subject. It made me smile as it was reminiscent of the
Nazi book burning bonfires...and had exactly the same effect. It just made
people more determined to succeed... It is interesting to note that the
Senior Management had no problem with my approach; but the Middle Management
felt threatened by anything that shook the status quo. Especially if it
might be seen to be achieving better-than-usual results.)

> >Read this carefully; when I use words I try to say EXACTLY what I mean...
> >
> >"Component technology is just ONE factor (albeit, a key one) that will
lead
> >to the removal of in-house computer programming for commercial
> >installations. There are many other factors and it is the COMBINATION of
ALL
> >these things that will render "computer programming" as we understand it,
> >redundant."
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> >It isn't just about technology. Businesses are starting to question the
high
> >costs of in-house IT. The result is a move towards packages like Siebel
and
> >SAP, coupled with outsourcing and buying temporary expertise for the
period
> >it is required (rather than putting these specialists on the corporate
> >pension fund...)
>
> That's what some of us do .. outsourcing and temporary expertise.

Yes, Robert, I know that. However, this forum at large is not composed of
freelancers. Neither is it a forum on ABAPS or Siebel Programming. Consider
yourself excluded from my discussion if you feel it doesn't apply to you
<G>. While we're on the subject, aren't you in the least concerned about the
rapid decline of the Freelance market and the savage cut in rates for COBOL
people? No, I guess at your age, you're not...

<remainder snipped as we agree>

Pete.
```

- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <3eceb533_3@corp-news.newsgroups.com> <3ecf9ad2.126388208@news.optonline.net> <3ed027f4_2@corp-news.newsgroups.com> <3ed16a8d.245120561@news.optonline.net> <3ed19ce9_2@corp-news.newsgroups.com>`

```
"Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>
>"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>is "something like" what was needed six months ago...)

Yep. This all agrees with my experience. 

The problem is, the system dampens feedback. It assumes planners will get
everything right the first time, thus there will be no errors to correct. In
fact, teams are disbanded as soon as their project phase is finished. If an
error is discovered later, the person who made it isn't even there! Programming
teams are disbanded as soon at the project goes into testing. As errors are
found, the test team corrects them because the programmers ae gone or
reassigned. Also, once a document is signed off by the users, you're not allowed
to correct errors in it. You just note the changes in the current phase's
documentation.

>RAD, on the other hand, encourages change as a means of evolving the system.
>New requirements and enhancements are welcomed and prioritised jointly with
>the Business, on the understanding that development is timeboxed and when
>the time is up, that's it.

Lots of feedback is a good thing. The process is self-correcting.

>> Users have been developing their own systems for years, using tools like
>Access.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>ability? History? Because "we're trained and they're not"?

The word trained makes people sound like circus animals. Let's say experienced.
We appreciate the importance of controls, checking return codes, backups, etc.;
amateurs don't. Their systems work as long as nothing goes wrong, but they
handle errors poorly or not at all.

>It may have escaped your notice, but the world has changed. The last place I
>worked (up until 2 months ago, so it is recent) there were more people with
>computer science degrees in the User area than there were in the IT
>department. Some of their "amateurish, control-and-edit lacking" systems
>were better than the stuff they got from IT.

Many CS graduates are mediocre programmers. The best programmers I've known were
nearly all self-taught.

>On the projects where we got to work together with them we were able to
>achieve some fantastic results, but I had the Devil's own job getting the IT
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>no risk, what can you lose...?

Many shops have a relaxed standard for small, low-risk projects. It is typically
used for one-time reports. It sounds like that one didn't.

>After the first one we had people in the business and IT queueing up to work
>on the next one.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>might be seen to be achieving better-than-usual results.)

To make such a major change work, you MUST have the support of Senior
Management. I've tried it with and without. The result was always success with,
mixed without.

>> That's what some of us do .. outsourcing and temporary expertise.
>
>. While we're on the subject, aren't you in the least concerned about the
>rapid decline of the Freelance market and the savage cut in rates for COBOL
>people?

Yes, VERY concerned. I used to say, "My security rests in the vibrancy of the
marketplace, not the good will of one employer." Well, the market isn't
vibrating like it used to.

Being a free market guy, I see the price of human labor determined like the
price of every other commodity: by supply and demand. Notions of 'intrinsic
worth' are simply wrong. Demand for COBOL continues declining, albeit slowly.
The big problem is on the supply side. The number of people is greater than the
number of jobs, and it keeps increasing. They are coming from CS programs, which
produce 25K graduates per year (US), and from overseas, principally India and
Russia. In 2000, the US increased the H1B quota for 'temporary, skilled' workers
from 115K to 195K (fewer than 100K of these slots were filled). Why, when we
have a domestic over supply? Because big business and big government pushed for
it, thinking an even bigger over supply would save them money. They were right;
it worked.

Moreover, worldwide networks have made it easy move the work offshore rather
than moving the worker here. There is no paperwork, no requirement for public
reporting, no government oversight. Wall Street firms have been pushing grunt
work to Bangalore, India, for several years. Within the last year, half the
(mostly clerical) work in my corner of cyberspace has moved to India and Manila.
Quality has suffered as a result (by documented, objective measure) but the
bottom line keeps improving.
```

```
problems for us. It is kind of ironic, but just after I left he applied for
a more Senior role and was turned down, much to his chagrin. Instead,
someone from the Business side who had been involved in  the pilots and was
very enthusiastic and supportive received the new post. I have no idea to
what degree this decision was influenced by the performance of the two
managers concerned, in work on our pilot projects. I made no comments about
either of them to any Senior Management (I make it a policy not to discuss
colleagues unless they are present, or I have spoken to them first) , but I
was pleased that the girl who got the job was selected.
```

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote in message news:<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>...

...Snipped the whole load of hogwash...

Bill has a lot of credibility here.  That means many of you reading
his post got instantly depressed.  If Bill says COBOL is irrelevant it
must be so.  I hope in the years I have participated here that I have
at least some level of credibility - and I'll tell you - it just ain't
so.

Here are some excerpts from my reply to Bills shotgun blast of email
to numerous email lists, vendor groups, standards bodies etc.  In
addition - some other replies in conversation I have had with others:

Hogwash.

Especially this part:

> question of "Is COBOL a dynamic/growing programming environment?" seems
> to warrant an equally strong "NO" as an answer.

There have been more new COBOL releases by a variety of vendors
RECENTLY
than in the same time frame 10 years ago. (say 1990-1993).   In the
last
few years the gap between what you can do with COBOL and what you can
do
with other languages has narrowed considerably.  It isn't because the
other environments are becoming static or shrinking - it's because
COBOL
is dynamic and growing.

Yes - COBOL Expo got cancelled (Postponed).  Don't blame COBOL.
***
I'm not going to get into a war with you.  I think you erroneously see
a
departure from COBOL - I don't see that at all - in fact I see failed
attempts at departure where in the end they RETURNED to COBOL and got
the
results they desired in the first place.  I have FIRST hand experience
to
the back this up.  The world if IT is still expanding - COBOL use is
expanding.  Considering the audience of this message, I'm not going to
speak to the Fujitsu specific issues - these are trade secrets and the
release of any such information could have an impact on future
competitive
advantage.  [NOTE: Bill asked several questions about Fujitsu's plans
for the future, the 2002 standard, staffing levels, etc).

But - while we are throwing wood on the fire.  Let's talk about the
Lattice C compiler or maybe Borland or Watcom?  I don't see COBOL
vendors
drying up at the rate I see C/C++ compilers going away.
***
You are just uninformed.  More below.

On 23 May 2003 at 18:31, William M. Klein wrote:

[Some Editing has occurred here for Accuracy]

> 2) As for compilers "drying up" think about:
> 
>   HP - no longer producing their own COBOL

HP UX COBOL Still around I think.  This I could be wrong on.  Doesn't
matter - it is ancillary to the death of the platform.

>   Dec - I believe they have "frozen" COBOL development

I work on a VMS system - we get regular updates to the compiler.

>   Microsoft - not "branding" their own or anyone elses

So what?  This is very old news.  

>   AcuCOBOL - still going but still without Intrinsic Functions

But with frequent releases and updates to their tools and with
additions
to new platforms as they come into being.  Extend 6 is about to be
released.

>   RM - Haven't heard of any new releases (but I could be wrong on this)

Just recently had a release and now support XML.

>   CA-Realia - Again no new releases or features (as far as I know)

CA-Realia is just about ready for a new release. It is still being
enhanced and updated.

>   IBM hasn't had a new release for AIX, VSE, or VM for close to 5 years
>   (I
> think)

But continually releasing updates for Z/OS.

> 
> ... but I do think that some people aren't
> being realistic about the current state of COBOL.

Those C compilers I mentioned just aren't anymore (save Borland but
you
can't "buy" it).

And we have NEW COBOL compilers:

PerCOBOL
Fujitsu
Fujitsu .NET
Fujitsu Linux 
KOBOL
Several other free/open source projects
MicroFocus will introduce a new COBOL compiler targeting .NET.

There's only one new C/C++ compiler I am aware of -> Intel.  As far as
I
know if you want to target Windows you have 3 choices.  GNU, Microsoft
and
Intel.  There's a dying market.  COBOL is not.
****
In another conversation with someone else...

Lamenting the lack of COBOL in the press, my reply:

This is nothing new.  There's never been a magazine for COBOL. (Except
COBOL report)  It's like having a magazine for concrete.  COBOL is so
mainstream and endemic that it doesn't get a "special" mention - it's
not new.  I don't think this matters.  Yes - schools don't teach it
much.  Every place I worked we
trained in house people to write COBOL.  It's an easy language to
learn
and to become productive with.

Concerning talk of conversions away from COBOL not TO COBOL:

I keep hearing of conversion from COBOL applications to C++ or Java
> etc., but never the other way around

OK - Well I just completed one that was a COBOL to VB - Failed, COBOL
to
C++, Failed, COBOL to the Web - Failed - COBOL to Modern COBOL -
Success!


Relative costs:

COBOL-> VB - $1,000,000 before the plug was pulled.
COBOL-> C++ - Several Million dollars
COBOL -> WEB - Hundreds of thousands (got smart faster) - Included an
oracle conversion.

Each attempt was an attempt to convert and modernize the application. 
Each failed to deliver.  My modernization has cost < $50,000 so far
and
delivers what each of these promised but failed to deliver.

The whole topic of language conversion is a red herring.  If I had a
good
working system written in C++ or Java, I would not convert it.

Here's another email I got, with identifying information removed:

Hi folks:

I just thought that everyone in the [Snipped for confidentiality]
would like to know that a major corporation that I
have been working with has cancelled a large project
($5,000,000) which used Java as the primary
programming language.

The company was unable to obtain acceptable
performance with Java when handling large data files.

The good news is that they decided to return to COBOL!

***
There will be New things in COBOL.  There will be renewed COBOL
Advocay - that I will be involved with.  The future is very very
bright.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

```
> The whole topic of language conversion is a red herring.  If I had a
> good
> working system written in C++ or Java, I would not convert it.

I agree with this pretty much.  However, conversion is not always for the
sake of conversion.  For example,
let's say you want to port your business logic into a J2EE environment using
the de-facto model-2 architecture (model-view-controller).  You're going to
use Struts.  I really can't imagine keeping COBOL around in this scenario...
if it's even possible.  Suddenly you're looking at a .net solution.  Code
reuse between .net and J2EE is possible, but not preferable.

>
> Here's another email I got, with identifying information removed:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> The good news is that they decided to return to COBOL!

You should probably email the guy back and tell him there is this "thing"
called the JNI.  Want to write file access in assembler for your native OS?
Go for it.  Want to do it in an applet?  There are ways to.  I suspect it
was not a language issue, but more likely a hardware issue; in many cases
modern-server boxes simply do not have the throughput of modern-mainframes.

--R
```

```
Now there's an interesting one to quote as an 'advancement', knowing your absolute aversion to COBOL having
anything to do with GUIs. XML is about putting stuff on a screen in Graphical representation - not DOS text
mode. Using three-tier systems, anything appearing on a screen is classified User Interface. In my book :-

compute XML = (Graphical) + (User Interface) !

Presumably RM are introducing something parallel to N/E's Forms Designer module for the Web ? But a problem.

you see Fujitsu, Hitachi or Micro Focus dropping their current modules. They can't - just too much 'legacy
code' already written. As a guess, it will be a dog's breakfast, (a) Introduce the new Standard but, (b) for
backwards compatibility, retain the 'old'; (c) third alternative a 'mix-n-match' of new and old- kiss any
dreams goodbye for portability and COMMON invokeable classes from one compiler to another.

I'll cover future Standardization later - but refer to it en route.

E-mailed him some sample OO code. (Could have walked across the back lane and thrown hard copy over his fence
<G>). To him absolutely unintelligible - bearing in mind he is into pharmaceuticals, gnomes etc. and not your
luverly Debits and Credits.

> Concerning talk of conversions away from COBOL not TO COBOL:

…[39 more quoted lines elided]…

Now it's the above I really wanted to get some background on.

With limitations, you can do almost anything in any language. Don't forget I could string a bunch of Wang  w/p
macros together and automatically give you a summary report, beautifully printed out on two pages in high
quality typewriter script. That is of course, if you were prepared to leave the machine on overnight while it
chugged away for eight hours to produce those two pages !

One I'd rally like to get a handle on - how 'Quick' or 'Slow' Java really is. The breed of books 'COBOL to
Java etc...",  just in passing refer to slowness. Then you read those URLs I've quoted above, plus your
correspondent's comments..... ?

Regarding the failures, which of the following apply, and it could be an absolute mix.

1. Director or senior management level - gets on an ego trip. He's going to 'change the world", picks language
du jour and dump COBOL.

2. Ditto # 1 above - but the Head of IT. Having singularly determined that's the route he's going, makes sure
he employs sycophants who buy into his dream.

3. Perhaps the most significant of the three - the technicians who will do the coding.

Side tracking, about learning languages, let's compare TYCI24H with Core Java Vol I, ignoring the introductory
chapters :-

TYCI24H

- Different Data Types
- Basic User Interface
- Procedure Division
- Manipulating Data
- Manipulating String Data
etc...

Core Java

- Data Types -  Integers, Floating Point, Character Type, Boolean
- Assignments
- Operators
- Strings
- Control Flow
- Class Methods
- Arrays

Now I *know* I'm biased, but when I'm thinking 'How do I use this with a Payroll" - I get a better feel from
the COBOL text as opposed to the Java text. By its very nature and name COBOL, I believe, gives me a much
warmer cosy feeling about business applications.

Do those young grads in the other languages have the savvy to implement good business systems. Doesn't seem to
me that the structure of learning lends itself easily to Debits/Credits - but it's great for Web paging. The
smart ones will of course pick up the techniques, although initially many will get seduced by the approach in
#'s 1 and 2 above. Similarly I see in M/F many much more proficient than I am at tweaking GUIs. Then I
question in my mind, 'Are they producing proficient, maintainable applications,  or are they produciing a
future nightmare for maintenance. (The last is not based on fact, but a gut feeling).

COBOL - outside of 'clerical business' - As Steve Burns pointed out, avionics at Boeing, or myself years ago
getting into Non Destructive Testing, (Corrosion Analysis) and a first quick one-off  testing patches of crops
raised on different fertilizers. Can't remember the specific name, but something like "Analysis of Variation
from Coefficient" (???))  Second quick one-off - summarizing cutting lengths , weights etc. required for Rebar
(Reinforced Steel Bars) used in conjunction with concrete for construction. Throw in another one, Richard's
three-dimenional graphics for loading a merchant vessel. Steve and Richard were probably working with COBOL 85
- I was working with COBOL 74.

In a sense COBOL is its own worst enemy. With something like 90% (??) being coded for the big iron, and the
associated staff costs (TREMENDOUS). It presented an opportunity for the SAPs, Siebels etc. to cast an
avaricious eye on a potential market. They have to have a generic name - but I think 'Monster Decision Table
Generators' is close. Expensive, but they can make serious inroads to in-house IT costs.

Locally an oil patch Treasury Department looked at a package, (wheeling, dealing, 'money futures', if you
like, both depositing and borrowing; lots of contact with WTC until 9/11 - sadly no more. Price tag between
$0.5 - $1 million and used by only SIX people in Treasury ! Horrendous - but not if at the end of one month
you can ensure that you don't pay a penalty of $1 million, or in fact can ensure you get interest of $1
million.

Seeing that he mentioned CPR, I'm guessing he's in Montreal, but It was Anon who wrote :-

"Once a business has converted most of its non unique processes to packages,
there's sometimes not a heck of a lot of legacy code left.  At that point,
the conversion process to "language x" on "platform x" doesn't seem so bad.
I'm not saying that's the way it always is, but I've seen it in several
instances.  And in those instances, COBOL got completely dropped because of
it -- even though I suggested COBOL remain in the Business Logic tier.  I
can only name one client that I've worked for: Canadian Pacific Rail, that
actually continued to use COBOL for its Business tier."

Whether or not a good business decision, I can see the thinking and the progression of the above happening.

Now back to a future Standard. And truly I am serious. Your word, the language is 'concrete'.  I recall Bill's
long list of enhancements in COBOL 2002 that would affect the folks on the big iron. My reaction - So ?
Naturally the language does need to be fine-tuned to keep abreast, but at the current time using COBOL
Minus-2002, PLUS the already well-worn extensions vendors have provided - we can get by quite happily.

I can't address the others - but from M/F's Answer Exchange. I wouldn't mind betting that 98% don't even know
J4 exists. I never see queries on "How to" .... EVALUATE, PERFORM, IFs, SECTIONS, GO TO etc. The questions,
other than some clarification of SQL and DB, are mainly in the realm of topics outside of the Standard -  GUIs
-  Webbing, Dialog System, Dialog Editor/GUI classes - if they expressed their desires it would be, "Give me
tools that make it easier for me to plaster things on the screen".

Their desires are in the area that the other OO languages already compete in successfully. Trained at
university in C++ or Java, that's the route the majority will go.
If you accept the argument that COBOL is superior it's going to take them a fair time to get burned before
they join the league of the converted. Meanwhile installations will have made the breakaway from COBOL either
successfully or disastrously.

> There will be New things in COBOL.  There will be renewed COBOL
> Advocay - that I will be involved with.  The future is very very
> bright.

I would sincerely like to think you are right.

Jimmy
```

#### ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** LX-i <DanielJSNOSPAM@Knology.net>
- **Date:** 2003-05-25T14:01:53-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ED11321.4000900@Knology.net>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
Rather than try to counter your personal opinion, I thought I would just 
post my own...

I believe that in the next 5 years, COBOL will continue to be the 
workhorse behind most all big business transactions.  Other environments 
will continue to grow (the PC-based databases such as Oracle, SQL 
Server, and MySQL; the web-based presentation languages, such as XML / 
XSLT, and Java; the PC-based Windows apps, using C#, VB, etc.), and I 
don't see a big role for COBOL, simply because it was late to the game. 
  As PECD has proclaimed (and demonstrated), pieces of code written in 
COBOL can be integrated into other applications as components, and there 
may be some future for COBOL in this regard.

However, big business still runs the world.  As has been written in some 
of the replies, to some extent COBOL has been stagnant, thinking that 
this new wave of PC-based stuff will be gone soon.

The thing is, I've seen many things done as of late scrambling to make 
COBOL part of the equation.  I know for our platform (Unisys 2200 
ClearPath), Unisys has come up with WebTS (Web Transaction Server), 
which allows users to access the same screens through the web that they 
would through a terminal emulator.  By placing "templates" for each 
screen, we can customize, do up-front edits, all through <insert browser 
of choice here>.  We can send up to 100,000 characters of text through 
messaging, and for larger outputs, we just write a file and send the file.

This one product allowed us to generate a "GUI" for our 20-year-old 
legacy system in less than a year.  (Now, if they'd let the web design 
gurus at them, they'd really be sharp, but for now, they're "web 
enabled" (GUI-ized).)  The next version (due by the end of the year) 
will support JSP and J2EE-compliant JDBC, and the latest release of the 
operating system provided JVM, within which the aforementioned Jxxx will 
run.

This is all great, but the problem is that this is 2003!  I wrote my 
first Java applet in 1997 (I think that was the year)...  I understand 
that large companies many times cannot embrace programming "fads" 
(although we -do- have an Ada compiler), but what this means is that 
instead of companies using their workhorse mainframes as web servers, by 
now they've gotten other hardware to do what they wanted, and are using 
some flat-file transfer of data to "refresh" their PC-based presentation 
system.

(This is not a knock on Unisys - I've heard similar slow-to-the-game 
stories about other vendors.)

For COBOL to become more visible, I think that the "COBOL community" 
(loosely knit though it may be) must do a better job of educating the 
decision makers.  It's difficult when said decision makers are being 
taught in college that COBOL is on its way out, but it is a very robust 
language that can do small things and/or big things quite easily. 
Whether this will happen or not, I'll tell you in 2008.  :)
```

#### ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** "anon" <no@no.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-27T01:48:13+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<xtzAa.9240$Fn1.2274109@news1.telusplanet.net>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
I'll throw in my 2 cents here.  I'm mostly a lurker, but I have COBOL
Unleashed and I recognize the author ;)

IMO one of the biggest things that seems to kill COBOL environments is the
"death of the mainframe" as businesses move to n-tier environments.  I say
that in quotes because I've never really seen any downsides to a mainframe
environment in a department that knew what it was doing <shrug>.  Over the
past 4 years I've seen clients chuck out their mainframe environments
because they didn't like the upkeep cost, or they didn't want to buy new
hardware when their maintenance contract expired, or they thought that
n-tier environments are the way of the future.  There seems to be an
overwhelming urge to get rid of everything on the mainframe when this
happens... including the COBOL environment.  Included in this process is the
tendency to move to package solutions, which can completely replace entire
legacy systems (ex: Peoplesoft, Oracle Financials/HR etc.).

Once a business has converted most of its non-unique processes to packages,
there's sometimes not a heck of a lot of legacy code left.  At that point,
the conversion process to "language x" on "platform x" doesn't seem so bad.
I'm not saying that's the way it always is, but I've seen it in several
instances.  And in those instances, COBOL got completely dropped because of
it -- even though I suggested COBOL remain in the Business Logic tier.  I
can only name one client that I've worked for: Canadian Pacific Rail, that
actually continued to use COBOL for its Business tier.

I'm familiar with Fujitsu and MicroFocus, so I know it's not just a
"mainframe" issue.  It might even be worth starting a topic like "COBOL in a
Windows environment" to get off on that tangent.  There I could probably
spend another 1,000 words.

I'm also going to throw this out there...  the number of languages that have
been developed from the ground up as OO languages seems to grow every few
years.  With C# and Java out there -- and their fairly extensive class
libraries to fall back on -- the OO versions of COBOL don't seem too great
to developers.  Especially when you consider the fact that you can throw
Java, C#, or (ostensibly) VB.net into almost any layer in an n-tier
environment.  Is OO really that great?  That's debatable too.



--R
```

#### ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** Steve Burns <"stephen.d.burns2 <at> boeing.com">
- **Date:** 2003-05-27T14:33:06+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
20 years ago I worked in avionics at boeing.  (avionics is the software
that runs an airplane.)  Avionics in thoise days was mostly written in
assembler.  ADA was new and at that time was considered to be the new
thing for avionics.  All programming was done by engineers, because
boeing did not think that computer scientists could understand the
complexities of the designs.  The programs were terrible (from a
computer science point of view.)  My job was to test them.  I tried to
convince a few of the engineers that they should allow some CS types
into their design sessions.  they just laughed.  Then I pointed out that
I could write their avionics programs in cobol faster and better than
they were doing in assembler.  They refused to believe that.

Most of the world (even many in the computing world) are as uneducated
about cobol as those engineers.  Most (even many who write cobol) don't
believe that cobol can be used as well or better as any other language
for almost any programming application out there.  

I have just spent the last decade in a maintenance environment.  I have
been modifying cobol code, some of which was written in the early 80's. 
I am sure glad is was written in cobol.  It has left me with a definate
impression about about the great need to have code that is easy to
understand.  We used to hear stuff about how much it cost a company to
maintain code as opposed to building it in the first place.  I don't
think I have any of that stuff for 10 or 15 years now.  

I am bewildered when I look at new code being written in C++, java, and
(shudder) net.data.  Where are all the lessons were learned 20 years
ago?  Companies are busily writing themselves into a maintenance
nightmere with these new languages.  How come no one in the cobol
community has tried to pont this out to company cio's?  It's already too
late, but it would be nice if someone tried.  (don't lok to me, I'm just
a flunky programmer.....)

"William M. Klein" wrote:
> 
>  J4 and WG4 posed the question "What <is> your vision for the COBOL language
…[80 more quoted lines elided]…
>  wmklein <at> ix.netcom.com
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

```
"Steve Burns boeing.com >" <"stephen.d.burns2 <at> wrote in message
news:HFJv36.L1v@news.boeing.com...
> 20 years ago I worked in avionics at boeing.  (avionics is the software
<snip>
>
> I am bewildered when I look at new code being written in C++, java, and
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>
It is quite natural for COBOL programmers to see it the way you describe.

The fact is that the world has changed and the arcane C++ and Java code you
see as unmaintainable won't be.

They can rewrite it quicker than they can maintain it. Most of it will be
"glue" for objects and components anyway. Have a chat to some of the guys
writing it and ask them about Objects and Components.

The use of Components and Objects (which both these languages support) means
that maintenance is minimised anyway as the functionality is encapsulated
into Object code. (as opposed to Source code...)

The days when the Holy Source Code was enshrined in the Temples of  COBOL
are coming to an end. Source code is actually becoming more and more
irrelevant, although people used to a lifetime of COBOL find this hard to
understand.

The maintenance nightmare you are concerned about is a wraith.

The reason no-one in the COBOL community has pointed it out to Management is
because few in the COBOL community understand the new technology, and those
that do, realise there is no problem.

Pete.
```

```
The whole world is becoming a world of disposable components.   People decry the
lack of skilled artisans, but it is cheaper to throw away and buy new.   Do you
darn your socks?   It costs $100 to have someone look at your $100 printer and
more to repair it.  They fix your car by pulling out computers and replacing
them until it works.   I haven't made a machine language patch to a program in
decades.   Only hobbyists and component designers look at the details.

Of course something as complicated as programming is going the same way.  (The
advantage of CoBOL as being highly maintainable isn't much of an advantage in a
disposable society).
```

#### ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-29T05:32:08+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<2934465.1054186328@dbforums.com>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`

```

Interesting discussion folks.... I thought I would throw my 2 cents in.
I think at least a few of you know me...
    The use of COBOL has diminished in the past 10 years more than it
    had in the previous 30 or so. In my own opinion, the reasons for
    this include:

1. COBOL is viewed as "un-modern". You will be  hard pressed to find
   many (if any) computer science departments teaching COBOL. Even many
   business schools are dropping it from their curriculum. This lack of
   fresh COBOL blood coming into IT is far more detrimental to COBOL
   than typically noted.

2. What kept COBOL popular previously was its ease of use, portibility,
   and ability to handle business problems. COBOL began to lose its
   ability to handle business problems when it failed to embrace newer
   technologies such  as GUI and later, the Internet, in a timely
   fashion. The COBOL vendors failed to deliver proper tools in a timely
   fashion (from day one) that allowed enterprises to develop best of
   breed applications on these new platforms - END OF SUBJECT.

3. The fact that COBOL compilers were selling for $3000+ against $99
   Visual Basic and C++ compilers also had something to do with this (a
   fact I seldom see mentioned...).

4. Object COBOL was a bust. It is interesting to consider the argument
   for Object COBOL over 10 years ago. C++ was a "natural" extension of
   OO to the "C" language and most "C" programmers became C++
   programmers. So it seemed to be a no-brainer that most COBOL
   programmers would become OO COBOL programmers and the COBOL market
   would continue down a successful path of modernization. Problem was -
   there was no compeling reason to use OO COBOL. This fact was made
   worse by the lack of OO libraries (such as GUI) with early deliveries
   of OO COBOL. And another fact seldom noted is the fact that OO COBOL
   was almost a completely different language - it seemed easier to
   learn by non-COBOL programmers. Additionally, it was (and  still is)
   far easier to convert typical "C" programs to "C++" programs than to
   convert legacy procedural COBOL programs into OO COBOL (and consider
   the additional fact that COBOL programs are typically muchg larger on
   average than "C" programs so the effort to convert them to OO was
   often greater as well.

5. In the PC environment, COBOL was never on a level playing field with
   the other PC languages. You often had to wait for a COBOL vendor to
   provide glue or wrapper code to give you a change at accessing some
   new technology such as COM that was available from day 1 with other
   major PC languages. You had to wait for the COBOL vendor to provide
   some unnatural highly abstracted GUI support, which was often kludgy
   at best and created applications that looked like no other in their
   native environments. You had to wait for the COBOL vendor to create
   database support in the form of an EXEC SQL precompiler and runtime,
   etc. Using COBOL simply became more and more of a chore as time
   progressed.

6. The COBOL committees themselves contributed to these problems by
   becoming mired down in  theoretical arguments over archane issues in
   OO and such and still refused to deal with pressing needs such as
   GUI. Now that they have FINALLY put out an OO spec (I believe some
   15+ year or so after taking  it up?) it seems a bit late -
   particularly when you consider that most vendors had long  since
   already implemented their own versions of OO.

..and so what does the future hold? I find it a real shame what has
happened to COBOL. If you look at some of the guiding principles behind
the initial invention of COBOL, some are still present. It is still the
easiest language to write applications in. And applications written in
COBOL do tend to work well and the performance of at least one PC
compiler is on par with C++ compilers. COBOL applications still run
faster and more stable than VB applications. Yet VB continues to grow in
popularity - primarily because it is easy to use and has available to it
all new technologies. And COBOL is still more portable than any other
language. Yet, this is becomming less of a factor as fewer and fewer
platforms are available (e.g. OS/2 is dead, many UNIX platforms are
going away, even the mainframe is dying off from a COBOL applications
perspective).
```
