```
And interesting observations from you, Howard II <G>. Some comments below :-

hhinman wrote:

> Interesting discussion folks.... I thought I would throw my 2 cents in.
> I think at least a few of you know me...
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Well there just appears to have been absolute rigid resistance to COBOL GUIs
- not just Thane but that's also the view of the propagandist for
Components, Pete Dashwood. (In case he's forgotten, I once wrote elsewhere,
"I think COBOL should have GUIs". And his response was, "Are you mad ?").
Wont develop comments about GUIs here - will do that in responding to Thane
about XML.

Worth noting though that perhaps 90% (???) coding is for mainframes, so J4's
thoughts would have been largely geared to that community. Never programmed
for mainframes but I'm guessing the 'green screen' is fairly close to Screen
Section ? Granted it's difficult to keep up with all the new gizmos - but
the significance of the impact of Windows etc. was lost

>
> 3. The fact that COBOL compilers were selling for $3000+ against $99
…[39 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Your reference to 15+ years - not quite. A triumvirate first proposed OO
COBOL - somewhere around 91/92, for which J4 formed the OOCTG. Their
deliberations were summarized in a paperback published in '93. But no
mention of GUIs, although they formulated the OO outline syntax which is
close to what we have to-day.

Nor at that stage did they consider Standard Classes. Around about 95/96
Russell Clarke, Australia, produced an exhaustively detailed paper on
collections/dictionaries etc. It was taken seriously enough to be passed on
to ISO(WG4) for comment - which they did. Then the damn thing was put on ice
until Bill Klein "resurrected" it about two years ago as a topic. Meanwhile
Russell could care less, he's happily off using Java down in the Antipodes.

It's interesting that some of the OO COBOL authors made reference to the
fact that Standard Classes were scheduled for '97. They weren't smoking pot,
they got that info from J4 people.

Meanwhile in introducing OO, vendors IBM, Hitachi and Micro Focus *did*
introduce their own versions of Standard Classes, the latter two using
Smalltalk as a model for both GUIs and collections/dictionaries.
Arranga/Coyle in '96, (the first *real* OO COBOL 'How to....'), itemized
each of the three vendor class hierarchies.

I can't recall whether it was you or Anon in Canada who made comment on the
number of support classes in Java. I'm not going to sit down and count, but
I'm guessing Micro Focus has 300 plus support classes. Bearing in mind the
other OO languages have *everything* as objects, that's not a requirement
for COBOL. We already have our bread and butter text/numeric handling, and
frankly, other than using objects as 'pointers' to classes, 'pictures' only
need to be turned into objects for display or communicating with other
languages. I would suggest the M/F approach is adequate.

One BIG PROBLEM - the mainframe community have taken to OO like ducks to
water. Don't make me laugh. That being the case, when OO was first mooted,
perhaps somebody should have been saying, "That's very interesting, you
wanting to introduce OO. How about gong off and doing your own thing ?".
Perhaps two version of COBOL - Mainframe and the 'Others' ?

Pete's observation is probably correct, 'OO has been welded on to existing
COBOL syntax". Because the prime users of COBOL are mainframe, there has
also been an insistence that COBOL *shall* be vanilla flavoured,  - don't
introduce anything which is specific to an OS.

(I hate that word *shall* above, preferring *will*,  but J4/ANSI/ISO  have
determined *shall* is the word to be used in their documentation.)

Assume there was something called PC COBOL (i.e. it specifically produced
syntax for OO, totally separate from mainframe offerings). Just follow the
possibility through with the following code :-

1. As I do it at the moment, bearing in mind this is already paraphrased
using M/F's 'exclude the red tape' feature  :-

*>-----------------------
Method-id. "addAnumber".
*>-----------------------
Local-storage section.
01 ls-value CONSTANT            value 22.
Linkage section.
01 lnk-InValue                            pic 9(03).
01 lnk-OutValue                          pic 9(03).
Procedure Division using lnk-Invalue returning lnk-OutValue.
 add ls-value to lnk-InValue giving lnk-OutValue
End Method "addAnumber".
*>----------------------------

2. 'C-ify' the above by having those braces. I'll leave out the comment
lines which are my personal preference :-

{ Method-id. "addAnumber".
Local-storage section.
01 ls-value CONSTANT            value 22.
Linkage section.
01 lnk-InValue                            pic 9(03).
01 lnk-OutValue                          pic 9(03).
Procedure Division using lnk-Invalue returning lnk-OutValue.
 add ls-value to lnk-InValue giving lnk-OutValue
End Method "addAnumber". }

3. The above, although different from non-OO, would leave most COBOLers
reasonably comfortable. But give it an additional twist - some of the words
are optional just like the M/F "red tape" feature. So you use the above
syntax, and when you are comfortable in OO, you can do it 'shorthand' as
follows :-

{ "addAnumber".
01 ls-value CONSTANT            value 22.
Linkage section.
01 lnk-InValue                            pic 9(03).
01 lnk-OutValue                          pic 9(03).
Using lnk-Invalue returning lnk-OutValue.
 add ls-value to lnk-InValue giving lnk-OutValue
 }

Note on the above. If a variable appears before the word 'Linkage' then it
hs to be 'Local'. Similarly, following that non-OO pattern, why do I have to
include 'End Method "addAnumber"' - that's understood by the closing brace.
For God's sake don't suggest indented levels of braces !!! The only caveat
on the above, don't get too clever with the 'shorthand' - otherwise you
might just as well code in C++ <G>.

>
> ..and so what does the future hold? I find it a real shame what has
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> perspective).

I understand what you are saying about VB although I've never used it. It
has to be close to ten years ago since I had the Borland Suite which at the
time included WordPerfect, Paradox, Pascal etc... (Those were the days when
you fed some 30 diskettes to the monster to load the complete suite).

I only used WordPerfect and Paradox, but I thought their concept of 'screen
design' with pop-ups, properties etc. was just great and EXTREMELY
user-friendly. No doubt this is where VB picked up the baton and just kept
running.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-05-30T17:27:31+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ed72648_4@news.athenanews.com>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <2934465.1054186328@dbforums.com> <3ED676D3.45FF958C@shaw.ca>`

```

"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote in message
news:3ED676D3.45FF958C@shaw.ca...
>
> Well there just appears to have been absolute rigid resistance to COBOL
GUIs
> - not just Thane but that's also the view of the propagandist for
> Components, Pete Dashwood. (In case he's forgotten, I once wrote
elsewhere,
> "I think COBOL should have GUIs". And his response was, "Are you mad ?").

Neither Thane nor myself are opposed to GUI applications being written in
COBOL. (The problem is in providing a standard GUI interface as part of the
COBOL language).

I do recall the conversation you allude to, well enough to remember the full
context which you haven't quoted here.

You were proposing that the COBOL language should have an integrated
facility (a la Screen Section) to produce GUI controls in a platform
independent manner like the rest of COBOL.

You had not considered that GUI programming is event driven, and
diametrically opposed to the normal procedural coding in COBOL.  In COBOL,
YOU decide when you will send output or receive input (READ and WRITE); in
GUI, the user clicks a button and you better activate some code to deal with
it... It is a completely different paradigm.

Neither had you considered that the manifestation of GUI controls needs to
be controlled by a message servicing loop, or that different OSes and
platforms would need to support GUIs in different ways.

To you, it was just a pretty box that you could put stuff in.

Should be no problem for a COBOL vendor to implement...

That was a long time ago, Jimmy. Since then you have learned a lot more
about GUIs and the use of them. We have products like Bob Wolfe's Plexus
SP2, Fujitsu's PowerCOBOL, MicroFocus' Dialog, and even MicroSoft's MFC
which can all be linked to and driven by COBOL. I hope you wouldn't
seriously suggest that COBOL should still provide a "GUI Section"?

> Wont develop comments about GUIs here - will do that in responding to
Thane
> about XML.
>
> Worth noting though that perhaps 90% (???) coding is for mainframes, so
J4's
> thoughts would have been largely geared to that community. Never
programmed
> for mainframes but I'm guessing the 'green screen' is fairly close to
Screen
> Section ? Granted it's difficult to keep up with all the new gizmos - but
> the significance of the impact of Windows etc. was lost

Again, you are venturing into areas you know nothing about and making
assumptions based on what you DO know. (We all do this to some extent, at
some time, but it SHOULD be avoided...<G>)

"Green screen" programming on mainframes is NOTHING like using a Screen
Section. The facilities are provided by a subsystem (usually CICS or
similar) and COBOL interfaces to the screens through buffers that map to the
data on the screen. I would be surprised if the majority of mainframe
programmers have ever heard of (let alone used) a "Screen Section". The
screen formatting attributes are prepared outside COBOL, as a separate
exercise, producing a separate module that is accessed at runtime. It has
nothing to do with COBOL.
>
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I can immediately think of several reasons why they didn't do that. But
then, I am a cynical misanthrope who thinks no good of Standards
Committees...(at least when it comes to COBOL <G>)

> Pete's observation is probably correct, 'OO has been welded on to existing
> COBOL syntax".

I did not mean this to be disparaging. It is a fantastic engineering job
that was done by the architects of OO COBOL. However, because COBOL is and
always will be, COBOL, OO COBOL is verbose compared to other OO languages.
The reason for this is that the OO facilities have indeed been welded on to
the existing language. (I like to think of it as "Jake the Peg" with his
extra leg, but the humour of this allusion is probably lost on North
American readers who may never have heard of Rolf Harris...<G>)

> Because the prime users of COBOL are mainframe, there has
> also been an insistence that COBOL *shall* be vanilla flavoured,  - don't
> introduce anything which is specific to an OS.
>
Another reason why your "GUI Section" isn't going to happen...


>
> { "addAnumber".
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> hs to be 'Local'. Similarly, following that non-OO pattern, why do I have
to
> include 'End Method "addAnumber"' - that's understood by the closing
brace.
> For God's sake don't suggest indented levels of braces !!! The only caveat
> on the above, don't get too clever with the 'shorthand' - otherwise you
> might just as well code in C++ <G>.
>

I was interested to see this Jimmy. It is not a bad idea. Why not submit it
to J4... when they get through with RW's Logical Operator  symbols they
could have a go at this...<G>.

Pete.
```

```
Well Howard I bought your CDs and I figured I might as well be bogged down
closer to the bleeding edge so tonight I was looking through the booklet
that came with the CDs.

From the Introduction(with some minor editing by me):

"NetCOBOL for .NET comes in three packages: Professional($1800),
Developer($3300), and Universal($4200).

The Professional package contains the .NET supporting components and only
supports the development of applications that will execute in the .NET
environment. (From the back of the book: functions that are currently _NOT_
supported such as SORT/MERGE or ASSIGN TO PRINTER)

The Developer package adds Fujitsu COBOL V6 Standard Edition product and
multi-threaded run-time, giving the ability to develop native Windows
applications as well as .NET-targeted applications and integrate the two
together.

The Universal package is a combination of the NetCOBOL for .NET
Professional product and the Fijitsu COBOL V6 Enterprise Edition, giving
you our top-of-the-line Windows application development environment along
with the leading-edge NetCOBOL for .NET  product."

-------<RANT ON>---------
Well I guess COBOL in now on a level playing field, except for price. Maybe
when I see all of the neat things that I can do with the Professional
version I may feel differently, but to pay $1800 and NOT be able to develop
any native Windows apps and NOT have SORT/MERGE and to NOT be able to print
to a printer.........well the only adjectives that come to my mind might
well get me sued for libel and anyway they don't seem nearly strong enough!
This kind of pricing really puts people off.

I still think if Apple had priced their computer competively with the PC it
would be a much different world computing wise. Uncompetitive pricing
bespeaks of hubris and arrogance, the old we can charge much more because
our product is Sooooooooooooo much better. Yuck! Yeah I know it is "highly
integrated" as though other products aren't. Yeah I know it is only those
pesky individuals that complain, companies actuall don't mind being ripped
off.
-------<RANT OFF>---------

None of the above is directed at you personally. I really could not decide
where in this thread to place my comment and decided to put it here because
you produced the CDs. Further down in this thread Chris Richardson mentions
J2EE/JAVA. For a programmer intending to work on the mainframe into the
future this "might" be a better choice of what to learn. It certainly seems
to be the way IBM is pushing with COBOL interoperabilty with JAVA and use
of the Java Native Interface (JNI).
```

```
> > >    many (if any) computer science departments teaching COBOL. Even
many
> > >    business schools are dropping it from their curriculum. This lack
of
> > >    fresh COBOL blood coming into IT is far more detrimental to COBOL
> > >    than typically noted.
> > >
> > > 2. What kept COBOL popular previously was its ease of use,
portibility,
> > >    and ability to handle business problems. COBOL began to lose its
> > >    ability to handle business problems when it failed to embrace
newer
> > >    technologies such  as GUI and later, the Internet, in a timely
> > >    fashion. The COBOL vendors failed to deliver proper tools in a
timely
> > >    fashion (from day one) that allowed enterprises to develop best of
> > >    breed applications on these new platforms - END OF SUBJECT.
> > >
> > > 3. The fact that COBOL compilers were selling for $3000+ against $99
> > >    Visual Basic and C++ compilers also had something to do with this
(a
> > >    fact I seldom see mentioned...).
> > >
> > > 4. Object COBOL was a bust. It is interesting to consider the
argument
> > >    for Object COBOL over 10 years ago. C++ was a "natural" extension
of
> > >    OO to the "C" language and most "C" programmers became C++
> > >    programmers. So it seemed to be a no-brainer that most COBOL
> > >    programmers would become OO COBOL programmers and the COBOL market
> > >    would continue down a successful path of modernization. Problem
was -
> > >    there was no compeling reason to use OO COBOL. This fact was made
> > >    worse by the lack of OO libraries (such as GUI) with early
deliveries
> > >    of OO COBOL. And another fact seldom noted is the fact that OO
COBOL
> > >    was almost a completely different language - it seemed easier to
> > >    learn by non-COBOL programmers. Additionally, it was (and  still
is)
> > >    far easier to convert typical "C" programs to "C++" programs than
to
> > >    convert legacy procedural COBOL programs into OO COBOL (and
consider
> > >    the additional fact that COBOL programs are typically muchg larger
on
> > >    average than "C" programs so the effort to convert them to OO was
> > >    often greater as well.
> > >
> > > 5. In the PC environment, COBOL was never on a level playing field
with
> > >    the other PC languages. You often had to wait for a COBOL vendor
to
> > >    provide glue or wrapper code to give you a change at accessing
some
> > >    new technology such as COM that was available from day 1 with
other
> > >    major PC languages. You had to wait for the COBOL vendor to
provide
> > >    some unnatural highly abstracted GUI support, which was often
kludgy
> > >    at best and created applications that looked like no other in
their
> > >    native environments. You had to wait for the COBOL vendor to
create
> > >    database support in the form of an EXEC SQL precompiler and
runtime,
> > >    etc. Using COBOL simply became more and more of a chore as time
> > >    progressed.
> > >
> > > 6. The COBOL committees themselves contributed to these problems by
> > >    becoming mired down in  theoretical arguments over archane issues
in
> > >    OO and such and still refused to deal with pressing needs such as
> > >    GUI. Now that they have FINALLY put out an OO spec (I believe some
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> > > happened to COBOL. If you look at some of the guiding principles
behind
> > > the initial invention of COBOL, some are still present. It is still
the
> > > easiest language to write applications in. And applications written
in
> > > COBOL do tend to work well and the performance of at least one PC
> > > compiler is on par with C++ compilers. COBOL applications still run
> > > faster and more stable than VB applications. Yet VB continues to grow
in
> > > popularity - primarily because it is easy to use and has available to
it
> > > all new technologies. And COBOL is still more portable than any other
> > > language. Yet, this is becomming less of a factor as fewer and fewer
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
```

#### ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-29T05:41:37+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```

<I seem to have hit some key sequence that caused the post of my
previous message before I was finished - so here's the remainder>

.. So what do I think about the future of COBOL? Well, this will
certainly be controversial, but I believe the future of COBOL lies with
the success of Microsoft's .NET architecture.
    And why do I think this? Quite simple of course - because .NET is
    the ONLY place where COBOL is actually evolving. Both Fujitsu and
    Micro Focus are investing in .NET. And  from day one (at least with
    the Fujitsu Compiler),  COBOL is suddenly on a level playing field
    with the other Microsoft Languages. You can use the same screen
    painter for creating GUI applications, you can use the same Web
    Forms painter to paint Web applications, it's a snap to create COBOL
    web services, etc.
   In fact, .NET may have brought something to COBOL that the COBOL
   vendors themselves forgot about - class libraries - and about 5,000
   or so of them in fact. And now you can sit in COBOL and quite easily
   do just about anything possible to do in these other languages.
   the only real question is "Is it too late for COBOL?".  Many COBOL
   shops have spent the past 10-15 years migrating away from COBOL
   because of the failure of the COBOL vendors to provide them with
   proper tools to build their next generation of applications. These
   tools are now here in the .NET realm you can indeed build world class
   applications in COBOL. So how many will try.....?

-Howard HInman
```

```
Howard, Regarding your last point (regarding the future of Microsoft
.NET and it's impact to the future of COBOL.) - I could not agree more
with you.

<Warning. What follows may seem like yet another attempt to selfishly
promote my book. If this bothers you, please do not read any further.>

Howard, your point mentioned in your last post underscores the reason
why I wrote my recently published book (COBOL and Visual Basic on
.NET: A Guide for the Reformed Mainframe Programmer). In fact, that is
also the reason why I've decided to mention my book every chance I get
(and the reason that I was looking forward to attending and speaking
at the - now cancelled - COBOL EXPO).

Yes, as you mentioned, your statements will appear as "controversial"
to some. Here's another dose of "controversy"...I continue to promote
my book as being the only resource (at least in printed book form)
that completely supports a mainframe programmer's transition to .NET.
Ok, there are two other training sources. One of them is a well
regarded set of video training tapes (published by Fujitsu). In my
opinion, Fujitsu's training tapes are great and I highly recommend
this set of video training tapes. The other source is a book written
by Ron Reeves. In my opinion, Ron Reeves book serves a slightly
different purpose (different scope) than my book does. His book
appropriately introduces you to Fujitsu's NetCOBOL for .NET product
and .NET in general. On the other hand, my book also introduces you to
Fujitsu's NetCOBOL for .NET product...BUT then goes on to cover the
ENTIRE .NET technology offering AND all of the needed infrastructure
type knowledge (COM+, HTML, XML, XSLT, MSMQ, IIS, SQL Server,
Deployment, Windows Services, Debugging/Tracing, Visual Studio.NET,
etc.) that a Windows/Web developer would have a need for. Furthermore,
I've included Visual Basic.NET in each chapter - along side the COBOL
samples. I've even included a chapter for C# and J# languages (as a
primer). All of this and much more...hence, the reason the book is
nearly 1000 pages long <g> So, for now, I can comfortably say that my
book is one-of-a-kind.
 
I choose to promote my book, a resource that happens to support what
could be the Future of COBOL (in other words, the success of the
Microsoft .NET technologies). I'm doing this, not for my own selfish
gain, and not to upset (again) those that oppose the gesture of
"promoting", but simply because I care about COBOL. More importantly,
I care about the large community of mainframe developers that might
stand to benefit from learning about Microsoft's .NET technologies.

Please use either of the following links for more information: 

http://www.amazon.com/exec/obidos/ASIN/1590590481/eclecticsoftw-20

http://www.eclecticsoftwaresolutions.com

http://apress.com/book/bookDisplay.html?bID=112
```

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message news:<bb5nkh$opm$1@peabody.colorado.edu>...
> On 29-May-2003, richardson@eclecticsoftwaresolutions.com (Chris Richardson)
> wrote:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

I agree, $80 is alot (actually $55.99 on Amazon) for any book. 

As to your statement: "mainframe programmer...wanting to expand one's
COBOL skills..."

I'd rather put it this way "mainframe programmer...wanting to expand
one's development skills while building Windows/Web applications using
.NET..."

My book is certainly not designed/written to teach someone more
"COBOL" per se. Rather, my book will teach you how to use the COBOL
that you already know in the .NET environment - while taking full
advantage of the .NET Framework to build Windows and Web applications.
Plus, I felt that the supporting infrastructure topics were critical
to include in the book. I liken this to someone learning COBOL (on the
mainframe) but not learning JCL, ISPF, and VSAM at the same time.

As a bonus, you can easily learn Visual Basic.NET using my book. I'm a
strong advocate of being a multi-lingual developer.

Yes, I do answer the question "what is .NET" (in Chapter 2). However,
this is far from a basic question. Can you imagine someone writing a
book to teach mainframe technologies and including a chapter to answer
the question "What is mainframe programming?" Which topics would you
think should be included? Assembler? TSO? QSAM? Copybooks? IEBGENER
and IDCAMS? I think you get my point. While answering that "What is
mainframe programming?" question, COBOL might just be the glue that
flows from one chapter to the other - while demonstrating all of the
aspects of "mainframe programming".

My approach taken in the book is similiar...While answering the
question "what is .NET" is covered in one chapter, that one chapter
actually demonstrates that it really takes 20+ chapters (you'll notice
that Chpt 2 includes many forward cross-references) to completely
answer that "what is .NET" question.

I think that understanding most (other) .NET training material is
challenging -mainly because most of the material out there assumes
that the reader is already well versed in some Windows/Web type
technology. That really is why I felt that writing a book as I have
(from a mainframe programmer's point of view, using mainframe
analogies, etc.) would be of great value to the mainframe community.

- regards.
```

```

On 29-May-2003, richardson@eclecticsoftwaresolutions.com (Chris Richardson)
wrote:

> I think that understanding most (other) .NET training material is
> challenging -mainly because most of the material out there assumes
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> analogies, etc.) would be of great value to the mainframe community.

That is a difficulty.   In my experience companies don't let their CoBOL
programmers play with PC's and Web.  They're needed for "the important stuff".
So they have some other guys experiment, using whatever skills they learned in
school, until they get something working.  If this new thing becomes important,
there already is an infrastructure built up with the new guys, their tools, and
their customers.   Occasionally the two infrastructures get together to find
some way to copy data.
```

```
Well lets get real. Even if your book and Howard Hinmans's CDs are the
absolute best educational materials ever produced, no employer will hire
anyone just because they have read a book (and please note that I am _NOT_
claiming that anyone said or implied this). They want experience and to get
experience with NetCOBOL for .NET Professional requires an up front cost of
$1800. Maybe not too much for a company but definitely an obstacle for an
individual just trying to increase his/her marketability. Basically it is a
bet on the future and no one knows that with anything approaching to
certainty. Is the future going to be as you describe?, or is it in
components as Pete Dashwood proposes?, or is it both?, or will it be
something else?.  NetCOBOL for Windows Professional is $1500 so  the
component approach also has significant costs.  Gee maybe I should buy both
and double up my bet!

I personally have enjoyed this thread and I think it is great that we have 
these educational materials  available. The expense of the material is
small compared to the time to master the material and the cost of the tools
required to gain marketable experience. Even if you make this bet there is
no guarantee that you will be employable in the future. All you you have to
do is look at all the people trained in the lastest and greatest who are
unemployed due to the market down turn. Each individual must make his/her
own decision about this, and as much as I appreciate your optimism, I am
concerned that less experienced may be unduly influenced by it and not
consider all of the costs and risks.
```

```
I thought that was a very well-balanced post, Charles and you highlighted a
couple of entirely relevant points:

1. Nobody is buying theory. (That is not to say that that both Chris's book
and Howard's CDs are not of value. We all need to start somewhere, but we
should not believe that doing the course will in and of itself, guarantee a
job.)

2. The Marketplace IS in a state of flux at the moment with several
different approaches vying for supremacy. I believe components will win, but
it IS speculation. You are absolutely right there is a Market downturn at
the moment and it could be more profitable to look at the Market, rather
than what technical options are available. .NET is by no means an assured
"Winner of the Future"...yet.

In passing I would mention that it has been possible to easily build
components in COBOL since Fujitsu V 4.2. (Maybe you DON'T need to pay $1500
for admission to the playground; have a look on e-bay for versions 4.2 or 5
or, particularly, 6...).

Pete.
```

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** richardson@eclecticsoftwaresolutions.com (Chris Richardson)
- **Date:** 2003-05-31T23:30:49-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b11feff.0305312230.1478d792@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <2934582.1054186897@dbforums.com> <b11feff.0305291104.5a1d8f99@posting.google.com> <bb5nkh$opm$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <b11feff.0305292017.681f109c@posting.google.com> <01c327c7$c9262000$d1c2f943@chottel>`

```
"Charles Hottel" <chottel@cpcug.org> wrote in message news:<01c327c7$c9262000$d1c2f943@chottel>...
> Well lets get real. Even if your book and Howard Hinmans's CDs are the
> absolute best educational materials ever produced, no employer will hire
> anyone just because they have read a book (and please note that I am _NOT_
> claiming that anyone said or implied this). They want experience

Yes, usually this is one of those catch-22's. However, .NET may be one
rare exception. Simply, because how new .NET is. Employers are not
asking for years of experience with .NET. .NET has not been in
existance long enough. It's only been around for a couple of years.
This fact won't be true forever...but for now, working knowledge - in
your living room, in a class room, etc. is certainly good enough to at
least impress someone in a job interview.

> and to get
> experience with NetCOBOL for .NET Professional requires an up front cost of
> $1800. 

By the way, both Fujitsu (NetCOBOL for .NET) and Microsoft (VS.NET and
.NET) offer free 60 day trials for their products. Visit their
websites (http://msdn.microsoft.com/vstudio/productinfo/trial/default.aspx
and http://software.fsw.fujitsu.com/DownloadPlus/Download.html)

> Maybe not too much for a company but definitely an obstacle for an
> individual just trying to increase his/her marketability. Basically it is a
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> and double up my bet!

I actually believe that I'm in agreement with Pete Dashwood's
"component approach". You see, to use .NET, to leverage its Framework
class library, to use VS.NET, to use the Common Langauge Runtime
really is just about using components (even if I keep calling them
Classes). So, a full transition to .NET fully supports the reliance on
components - even components written in different languages. Consider
that "COM" development was all about components and now .NET is just
the next phase  - after "COM".

> 
> I personally have enjoyed this thread and I think it is great that we have 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

In fairness, for those with less experience, I think the following
statements may be helpful as well (to contrast with your own).

You said: "Even if you make this bet there is no guarantee that you
will be employable in the future."

I say: "Even if you DO NOT make this bet, there is no guarantee that
you will be employable in the future." To not make THIS bet, is at the
same time , to make A bet on something else.

You said: "All you you have to do is look at all the people trained in
the lastest and greatest who are unemployed due to the market down
turn. "

I say: "Given that so many people have gotten laid off - regardless of
skill and training - competition for positions is likely to be fierce,
cut-throat and unforgiving. What are you going to use to help push
your ONE resume to the top of the heap - among the many? Perhaps you
will use your impressive degree and/or technical certification. Maybe
you will include a picture on your resume to show how nice you look in
that new designer suit. Of course, your years of expereince should
raise the brow of your would-be interviewer. So, when ALL things are
equal...what's going to set you apart from the other candidates that
are equally tired of collecting unemployment checks? You will have to
answer this question for yourself."

I'm not saying that .NET is the only answer to this last question.
But, it does not take a giant leap of faith to see that it is part of
the answer (at least for Windows and Web development). The other part
of the answer includes the whole Java, J2EE, argument. The answer to
that last question will differ depending on what company you go to for
the job interview. If it's a Microsoft friendly shop - .NET knowledge
will strengthen your job interview.

Keeping your saw sharpened, staying current with technology does not
have to be about avoiding a lay-off. You can look at it as giving you
yet one more feather in your cap, one more paragraph (but not the only
paragraph) on your resume.

- regards.
```

- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <2934582.1054186897@dbforums.com> <b11feff.0305291104.5a1d8f99@posting.google.com> <bb5nkh$opm$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <b11feff.0305292017.681f109c@posting.google.com> <01c327c7$c9262000$d1c2f943@chottel> <b11feff.0305312230.1478d792@posting.google.com> <3ed9e117_3@news.athenanews.com> <01c32861$74adecc0$a093f243@chottel> <01c32862$253bfbe0$a093f243@chottel>`

```
"Charles Hottel" <chottel@cpcug.org> wrote 

> Also Microsoft seems to have a habit of continually changing their vision
> of the future and to my suspicious mind it seems more related to the influx
> of more profits than to actual real value.

In the first edition of Bill Gates 'The Way Ahead' there was no
mention of the Internet at all even though (or perhaps because) at the
time IBM's OS/2 came with internet connection and Unix sites used it.

The book was quickly updated and reprinted and MS rushed to the front
of the crowd waving a flag saying 'follow me'.

Of course MS's initial attempt was to subvert the internet and replace
it with MSN where only Windows machines would work.  This was MS's
normal business practice.  For various reasons (mainly Netscape) MS
failed to kill of the internet and replace it with their 'better'
vision and so they had to allow Windows users access to the real
internet, because that is what they were doing in spite of MS.

Subsequently MS has made attempts to control the internet through
other means, such as trading Windows advertising real estate on your
desktop (for ISP connections to be in issued Winows system) for those
ISPs to be _required_ to use non-standard HTML features so that IE is
required to view the pages. (see recent arguments with Opera and the
Swedish Chef edition for how this continues).

Later, MS introduce Passport as a means of MS becoming everyones
doorkeeper. (see Gartner group report on avoiding passport).

.NET is another case where MS is rushing to the front of the crowd and
trying to seem as if they started out as leaders.  Part of .NET is be
MS's version of Java and the enterprise tools built around it.

Another part of .NET will be DRM.  A 'vision' where all music, movies,
and other media are distributed through channels that are secure and
_paid_for_.  Where users don't buy a CD, they licence the ability to
listen or view on a per use or per player basis.

No more taking your CDs from your lounge to play in your car or at
your friend's house, unless you have bought a licence to do that of
course.

On the one hand it would cater for making movies and music available
'on demand' as a secure channel would (try to) prevent piracy and
would allow payment without Music shops and Video rentals or Cinemas. 
This means that all can be complete couch potatoes.  On the other hand
the existing distribution channels would go broke, or be restricted to
niche markets when MS replaces them with its own channels.

Will it work ?

Currently many see .NET as a stick to beat Java with.  Of course some
see Java as Sun's stick to beat Windows with.

Others may see DRM as a stick to beat _everyone_ with.  There are
attempts to make DRM compulsory in the US, this may make the rest of
the world go elsewhere, as they seem to be doing increasingly now.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** richardson@eclecticsoftwaresolutions.com (Chris Richardson)
- **Date:** 2003-06-01T17:55:19-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b11feff.0306011655.4a4ff6a3@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <2934582.1054186897@dbforums.com> <b11feff.0305291104.5a1d8f99@posting.google.com> <bb5nkh$opm$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <b11feff.0305292017.681f109c@posting.google.com> <01c327c7$c9262000$d1c2f943@chottel> <b11feff.0305312230.1478d792@posting.google.com> <3ed9e117_3@news.athenanews.com> <01c32861$74adecc0$a093f243@chottel>`

```
"Charles Hottel" <chottel@cpcug.org> wrote in message news:<01c32861$74adecc0$a093f243@chottel>...
> Yes  but is the future .NET for COBOL or .NET for/with some other language.
> VB and C# spring to my mind.
> 

Good question. While no one knows the future, here are my thoughts:

I believe that one of the reasons Microsoft designed the .NET platform
openly to support multiple languages (their own languages, plus those
of other vendors) is that they are staying true to the "we will build
a sand box, you can bring your own toys" approach. As long as you play
(and pay) in the sandbox, I don't think they really care what toys you
bring. They just don't want you to go play in the J2EE/Java sandbox.

Yes, on the mainframe side of the world, you have people wondering if
COBOL will survive on the other side of the fence. Note, that you also
have people wondering which language VB.NET or C# (or J#) will
survive. I believe that Microsoft leveraged their own bet - to them,
it really does not matter. Either way, VS.NET and the .NET Framework,
Windows, etc. survives.

This is where we (the developers) come in. Depending on use and
adoption (and vendor support!) all, either, or neither of the
languages could remain as part of the .NET picture.

Take Javascript, VBScript, and JScript as examples. Every web
developer that I know uses Javascript (a non-Microsoft product). Does
Microsoft care? My guess...not really. Sure, Microsoft offers their
own Client Scripting languages (VBScript and JScript ) that are
Internet Explorer specific. Still, Microsoft "loyal" developers still
migrate over to Javascript - all the while building Web applications
using Microsoft's ASP.NET toolset.

Developers made the choice...not Microsoft! All the deep pocketing
Microsoft Hype did not make the difference (in this case). VBScript
and JScript are still alive...but Javascript is King.

<off-the-wall analogy section>

To me, it's like going into the store to buy a bag of chips. I do not
think that the store merchant really cares what kind of chips I buy -
or whether I even buy any chips at all - as long as I buy "something".
let's say that I do buy a bag a chips...Likewise, I don't think that
"Lays" would really care whether I buy the "fried" or "baked"
type...as long as I buy something that carries the "Lays" seal.

As a consumer, If their is nothing in the store that I want, I will
not buy anything. It's up to the powers that be to make it worthwhile
to go into the store and purchase something. Sure, a few foolish
purchases will be made...but over the long haul, quality and customer
satisfaction will win.

</off-the-wall analogy section>

So, the .NET technology suite is just one huge sandbox. Time will only
tell which toys people will ask to bring in, attempt to bring in, then
insist on bringing in to play with. So, which language(s) will win the
hearts of the developers? I really hope that COBOL remains on the
list. It really is a good bridge between the two worlds. Hopefully,
the vendors (i.e. Fujitsu) will stay committed for the ride.

At the same time, I suppose you could say that I leveraged my bets as
well (by including both COBOL and VB.NET in my book (and as part of my
own career development). I realize that some mainframe developers want
nothing more than to code exclusively in COBOL - no problem. I wanted
to provide a mainframe-friendly book (to learn .NET) for that crowd.
At the same time, there are those mainframe developers that do not
want to code in the COBOL language any more (shame, shame, shame) - in
favor of some other language - maybe VB.NET. Well, my
mainframe-friendly book will equally speak to that crowd as well -
while they learn .NET.

(Note, I did include a C# and J# section in my book as well.)

Me, I'm part of a third crowd. As I've mentioned a few times, I
advocate being multi-lingual - flexible - still, while learning .NET.
For me, this seems like a safe position. I enjoy developing in multple
languages - it's fun. It's not trying to straddle both sides of the
fence...I see it as standing securely smack-dab in the middle of the
.NET fence.

Which language will survive as part of the .NET picture? Not sure. I'm
not convinced that Microsoft really cares. So, I'm leaning in the
direction of not making that my biggest priority. As you can see, I'm
more concerned about which "developer group/community" - while made up
of multiple "crowds" - will survive.
```

```
Well I agree that Microsoft may not care, but a programmer looking to
develop marketable skills does care, as it determines what he/she needs to
learn.

I entered into using PCs kind of late. At one point it looked like knowing
Unix and C++ would be a plus at my workplace. I already knew some UNIX from
getting my masters degree. So I signed up for a C++ course and bought
Borland's C++ compiler. I think it was version 5. Next I took a Windows
programming course and bought Visual C++ from Microsoft. Unfortunately the
course was very poor. At this point  my agency decided they did not have
the money to build and support the multi-tiered architecture they had
envisioned. Mutiple hundreds of millions down the drain with very little to
show for it, but that is another story. Meanwhile I could not help but
notice that the C++ compilers I had used became obsolete environments
mighty fast, replaced by newer versions or different application building
products/frameworks. 

Undaunted I bought Mike Murach's book on VB 5.0 and purchased MS VB 5.0. I
learned quite a lot even though my version of MS Access was already
obsolete and not the version used in that book. My timing was poor as
within two to three months VB 6.0 was on the market. To this point I had
always purchased the professional editions thinking I could learn more
features.

What is the point of all this long, tragic, and very sad story? Well I
realized that I simply could not afford to keep up with the latest and the
greatest. Instead of keeping myself employable I was just helping to make
Bill Gates and Microsoft rich. The lastest and greatest often were not so
much greater than what came before.

So I got smart and read Mike Murach's book on JAVA and the price was right.
But to me it seemed like just another way to do GUI and afterwards it
seemed that most JAVA coding was being done on the server side (so maybe I
was _NOT_ so smart after all).

Along the way I learned some HTML which was easy for me because I had a lot
of experience with typesetting languages. One book had a C program which
listened to port 80 and showed what the browser was passing and this
evolved into how to do some basic CGI programming. I read Wilson Price's
books on Elements of COBOL Web Programming and Elements of OO COBOL
Programming. Now as I mentioned previously I am learning more about
components.

So what is my point? After all of this I doubt that I am any more
employable than if I had not done any of these activities. Part of this may
be due to poor timing and poor choices on my part, but I will cut myself
some slack on that, as it simply shows poor aptitude in predicting the
future. The main shortcoming is that I never used any of this stuff at work
so I don't have real experience to offer. In the past I taught myself IBM
mainframe assembler and CICS and parlayed limited on the job experience
into real, extensive, and substantial experience. In todays environment
management wants and can get years of experience in exactly the computer
environments and languages that they need. The problem for someone trying
to maintain marketablity is that there are so many platforms, languages,
and products that it is an almost impossible job to decide what is best to
learn.

So if I learn .NET and COBOL and VB.NET I doubt that someone who wants C#
or whatever will even consider someone like me. I certainly am extremely
leery of betting multiple thousands of dollars on the prospect when I have
already bet multiple hundreds of dollars in the past to little effect. I
simply do not believe that the choice is as clear cut and as certain as you
make it out to be. Of course _NO_ choice is also a choice as you pointed
out, and learning something is better than nothing. Certainly I have
receieved the enjoyment of learning and all my effort was not in vain, but
it is a very difficult marketplace out there right now. Hell even if I were
to pick exactly right I might have to move to India to get a job for a
small fraction of what I make now. In my opinion the future is way less
certain than you make it out to be.
```

```
I do recall a few years ago, just as I was gearing up for the .NET
dive in acknowledging to a friend that I was going to dive in ï¿½ in
spite of the risk. At that time, I used Microsoft's statement about
how they where "betting the farm" as my justification for "betting my
own farm". Yes, my farm (a few hundred dollars) pales in comparison to
theirs (more money than I care to imagine). But, it's relative <g>

I've resolved to jump aboard the technology train as it speeds ahead.
I realize that it's going to change colors from time to time and will
visit different towns ï¿½ one after another. The only thing that is near
certainï¿½the train will not stop. That's one of the exiting things
about technology and being so intimately tied to it's changes.

As your own experiences prove, this is simply part of the package. I
would cut you tons of slack. I don't think it's your choices or
timingï¿½it's just the nature of technology. Take HTML for example, you
mentioned that you had learned a lot of it. Well, now, there's a
strong movement now to transition to XHTML (well formed HTML, like
XML). Does that mean that it was wrong to learn HTML? No, of course
not.

I've shared a few of these experiences as well (taking a VB5 class,
only to find out that it was obsolete before the class ended <g>).

At any rate, your points are well taken. I suppose my optimism for the
future mistaking comes across as if I'm certain about the future. I
think the only real safe prediction is that things will look very
different than they look right now.

These things just evolve. And that's just the way that it is (in my
opinionï¿½of course).

- regards.
```

```
Charles Hottel wrote:

A good one Charles, and Chris came back at you on a positive note.

Before typing this I checked the Saturday Careers Section of my local paper.
Not ONE advertisement for a programming job for *ANY* language. Just two ads on
the back page for learning from SAIT(Southern Alberta Institute of Technology)
and OOST(OO Software Technology) offered by the Universities of Calgary and
Regina. (Like the oil/gas patch in Texas, Alberta, and specifically what has
been dubbed 'the Calgary/Edmonton Corridor', is extremely high tech. About a
year back there was competition between Vancouver and Calgary for the title
'Silicon Valley North' - Calgary wins hands down). No we aren't in the doldrums
up here; at the moment the indicators are our economy is doing better than
yours, with an increase in the Canadian dollar value.

The computing market , (hardware and software) is just drying up. What puzzles
me though is that both Smalltalk and Java NGs have lots of active input - so
what the hell are they programming ? I previously mentioned my neighbour
working from home programming for his corporation in Australia in C++ - but he
is into a very SPECIFIC niche market.

At the high end - SAP, Siebel etc., have attacked the traditional number
crunching market. How about sitting down and writing an accounting application
for a PC. Forget it - why even attempt to compete against products like
Intuit's QuickBooks. A savvy user can apply their package at a fraction of the
price of a product you can produce - and that's just putting the meagre crumbs
on your plate.

Somewhere in between the two, SAP which is just too damned expensive for them,
and Quickbooks which although excellent just doesn't contain the additional
whistles and bells needed, there is a middle market - but that is a difficult
one to find.

Am I glad I'm not just starting my career in this racket ! To succeed you have
got to find that speciality niche, regardless of which language/tools you are
using. (I couldn't help but smile - I don't really think writing a nifty
routine in .NET to compress/zip your files really counts - unless of course you
are hoping to knock Winzip off the shelf <G>).

Jimmy
```

```
"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote in message news:<3EDBF0BB.4549EC2D@shaw.ca>...

I read the above response...and just as I was thinking that there were
several LOCALE type references ... ones that I have not the pleasure
of really understanding and appreciating...I noticed one word that I
do understand dearly.

"chuck"

This is the first time in a while - since I left my home state of
Texas and headed to sunny California that I've read/heard someone use
the word "chuck" while not referring to steak or the Rugrats cartoon.
In Texas, we used that term to mean "throw". It looks like you are
using it in the same way. How refreshing! (and I'm not being sarcastic
or disrespectful).

By the way...The notion of LOCALE...reminds me of an article read
recently that discussed the concerns of using a date reference and the
various other LOCALE concerns. For example (as I recall), a reference
of 9/11 to some may mean Sept 11th and to others it is Nov 9th or
better put as 11-9 if it is to really be meaningful...or something
like that.

Oh well...chuck that one to the fire <g>
```

- **Message-ID:** `<3EDCD59B.342EF681@shaw.ca>`

```


Chris Richardson wrote:

> "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote in message news:<3EDBF0BB.4549EC2D@shaw.ca>...
> >
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
> Oh well...chuck that one to the fire <g>

I think the use of words and there origins is fascinating. I envy both Chuck and BIll  who
said they did linguistics at university.

"Chuck" I use exactly as you did in Texas. But that's 'English' usage; I don't know if it
is a Canadianism. One of the Aussies or Kiwis would have to confirm, but I wouldn't mind
betting they say, "Chuck another steak on the barbie".

Your reference to dates - "9/11" is an Americanism which is scorched into the mind just
like December 7, 1941. Had the WTC disaster occurred in UK (God forbid !), I'm not sure how
it would have come out - "11/9" perhaps but not necessarily so. I think it would be 'so
un-English' to do it that way.

For an English mind parsing N.American dates it is a bit of a problem :-

04/05/2003 or 05/04/2003 - in isolation I don't know whether you are talking about May or
April. Given 04/13/2003 - then I've got it figured. It's even more difficult in Canada
because we use both dd/mm and mm/dd. For at least the last thirty years, whether in
programming or private usage I go with the ISO format : ccyy/mm/dd.

The English 'style' used to be, when I was shorthand/typing, to specify days with a suffix
:-

1st, 2nd, 3rd, 4th through 20th, 21st..... and so on....

So :        31st May, 1999

The other one I get caught on is N. American usage of 'hundreds' instead of 'thousands' -
although on BBC World News the other night I heard "fifteen hundred".

The one which really blows my mind, when referring to stocks/shares, "The market dropped by
half of one percent". Jeepers ! Why can't you folks say, "The market dropped by Oh point
five percent".

Military is a good focus point - "Friendly fire". Not too much "friendly" about being fired
upon ! I picked up from Gulf War II that the British Army uses "Blue on blue". Harkens back
to when the good guys were 'blue' and the Soviets were 'red'.

Dump is an interesting one. I think its general usage in N. America is 'to defecate' or 'do
a dump'. Entirely different meaning in UK. "Take the rubbish to the dump (landfill)", or
"Dump the leaves from the wheelbarrow over there".

Chuck (that's "Charles" not "throw" <G>), discussed the origins of the word "island" with
somebody, and "isle" came into the text. I can immediately think of the Isle of Man, or
looking southwards from my hometown Portsmouth, the Isle of Wight some 5-8 miles south..

Even Bill, who is somewhat 'trans-Atlantic', having worked in UK, recently queried Pete and
myself on English usage <G>.

Jimmy
```

```
"William M. Klein" wrote:

> I had to read thru the other replies to this thread before posting to see if
> we had any Bette Davis fans. For "authentic" American usage, you may
> download and LISTEN TO:
>
>    http://www.meredy.com/bettedavis/bd-dump.wav

Could be a problem with Netscape. All I got was the "player" in the centre of
the screen and the one and only phrase "What a dump !".  Couldn't even recognize
it was Bette D. Do people get paid for this ?

Take a look Pete you could make zillions out of selling simple one-phrase sound
Components ! No Chris - I'm not about to suggest he should use .NET to do it
easier. Mind you he might - $1 times a zillion ain't no chikenfeed <G>

Bill - seeing as you are listening to those book tapes. Ever thought back to the
stars of the '30s/'40s in the States. Accents have changed dramatically since
then. They were from the THEATAH - much more dramatic and precise. That's why so
many of them were able to transition to English parts in movies - their accents
were credible. Bette for instance as QE1 in "Elizabeth and Essex", along with
Errol Flynn (an Aussie, I believe).

To-day by comparison, some don't even try. Kevin Costner as Robin Hood - you
gotta be joking.

But back to the late '30s one of my 'funny' favourites an English 'Technicolor'
- Sabu, Raymond Massey (Canadian) and others in "The Drum". The jolly old Empire
in India. As you can imagine Sabu didn't have too much problem sounding like an
Indian (East). Raymond Massey turned in a good performance as the villain, with
his menacing tones he could put on. By contrast all his supporting villains were
from the English Theatah. They were dressed as Indians, turbans, beards the
works and used shoe polish to tan up to the local colour. But their dialogue
pronunciation, oh my gawd :-

"I say sahib old boy. Kind of hot. What ? What ?".

Jimmy
```

```
On  4-Jun-2003, "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:

> Well for sure it wasn't "The Viking(s)" - Tony, Kirk Douglas and TC's
> future./ex-wife Janet Leigh. Although it's a fair bet TC uttered some immortal
> phrases even in that one. I wonder where the Bronx village is in Scandinavia ?

I still think it is funny when people accept some modern accents in a historical
drama but not others.   In Sparticus, the Roman elite all had upper class
British accents (an accent that is at most 200 years old), while the slaves all
had American accents.

People didn't like it that Kevin Costner spoke with an American accent in Robin
Hood.  But experts guess that the people who speak closest to the way Robin Hood
spoke are in America, not Britain.

Some people even complained about Sean Connery speaking with a Scottish accent
in /Hunt for Red October/ .   But that couldn't be more appropriate since he
wasn't playing a Russian - he was playing a Lithuanian(?) who spoke Russian - in
English, so having an acceptable British Empire non-English accent (particularly
of one associated with engineers) worked perfectly.

Of course they could have Jesus speak with a New York City Yiddish accent - to
indicate that he was a Jew.   No sillier than having him speak with an upper
class educated accent.
```

```
>
>The Magen David did not become the universally recognised symbol of  Judaism
>until the 19th century, but its use by Jews goes back much further than

Hence the moniker of the 'Seal of Solomon' (which can be found, as I 
recall, in Burton's translation of '1,001 Arabian Nights' as the seal of a 
bottle which contains yet another djreadful djinn).

Speaking of 'Magen David'... one of the various dialects pronounces this 
as 'Moh-gehn Day-vihd', which was (and still is) the name of a sacramental 
wine... but when the alcohol content was changed from 12% to 20% and the 
name became less religiously associated it sold briskly as 'MD (or 
'MadDog') 20/20'.

>
>Although I am not Jewish myself, I have a number of friends who are, and
>most of them (especially the females) have  at least one of these in their
>possession and/or wear one most of the time. So whatever it may symbolise to
>others, Jewish people are pretty passionate about it...

Gah... in your experience, related above, *some* are 'pretty passionate' 
about it; I'd imagine (based on the Ancient Wisdom of 'three Jews = five 
opinions = seven political parties') that some are not.  Perhaps your 
acquaintances merely live in fear of vampires?

>
>This is the best reference I could find on Google:
>http://www.menorah.org/starofdavid.html

From the abovementioned site:

--begin quoted text:

The evolution of the six-pointed Jewish star, the "Magen David," literally
the "Shield of David." also known as the hexagram, or more rarely,
*Solomon's Seal, is long and complex. Although it is now the most common
and universally recognized sign of Judaism and Jewish identity, both
within and outside of the Jewish community, it has only achieved this
status in the last two hundred years. Before that it was chiefly
associated with magic or with the insignia of individual families or
communities. 

--end quoted text

... which is kinda close to my earlier recollections about 'the Seal of 
Solomon did not become a popular symbol of Materia Hebraica until the 
mid-19th century' and the bottle-seal of Great Power... and a bit farther 
down, same page

--begin quoted text:

In the Middle Ages, hexagrams appear frequently on churches, but rarely in 
synagogues or on Jewish ritual objects. It was the *menorah that served as 
the primary Jewish symbol from antiquity until the post-Renaissance 
period, not the "Jewish star."

--end quoted text

... and that seems kinda close to what I said with '... and that prior to 
that the most-recognised symbol was the multi-branched candelabrum (as 
seen on Trajan's Arch)'.

My memory is, admittedly, porous.

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT : American and English usage

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com

```
In article <3EDCD59B.342EF681@shaw.ca>, James J. Gavan <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:

[snip]

>"Chuck" I use exactly as you did in Texas. But that's 'English' usage; I don't know if it
>is a Canadianism. One of the Aussies or Kiwis would have to confirm, but I wouldn't mind
>betting they say, "Chuck another steak on the barbie".

It seems that a common colloquial use for 'chuck' is a variety of 
Englishes is 'to toss/throw casually'.

>
>Your reference to dates - "9/11" is an Americanism which is scorched into the mind just
>like December 7, 1941. Had the WTC disaster occurred in UK (God forbid !), I'm not sure how
>it would have come out - "11/9" perhaps but not necessarily so. I think it would be 'so
>un-English' to do it that way.

A few decades back I took the ferry from Calais to Dover and ran into a 
rather... snide Customs official who looked at my paperwork and said 
'What's this for a birthdate?... oh yes, you're American, some day you'll 
learn to write dates correctly.'

My response was 'Shortly after you learn to drive on the correct side of 
the road, perhaps.'

[snip]

>The English 'style' used to be, when I was shorthand/typing, to specify days with a suffix
>:-
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>So :        31st May, 1999

That's the style I use (but with cardinals instead of ordinals, eg. 31 May 
1999) after having been taught it by the Air Force... as long as one is 
familiar with the English names of the months it is utterly unambiguous... 
doesn't sort very well, though.

>
>The other one I get caught on is N. American usage of 'hundreds' instead of 'thousands' -
>although on BBC World News the other night I heard "fifteen hundred".

Isn't that the year between 1499 and 1501?

>
>The one which really blows my mind, when referring to stocks/shares, "The market dropped by
>half of one percent". Jeepers ! Why can't you folks say, "The market dropped by Oh point
>five percent".

News-speak is not always indicative of how 'you folks' speak; folks I hang 
around with say things like 'likely to rain' and not 'probability of 
precipitation'.

>
>Military is a good focus point - "Friendly fire". Not too much "friendly" about being fired
>upon ! I picked up from Gulf War II that the British Army uses "Blue on blue". Harkens back
>to when the good guys were 'blue' and the Soviets were 'red'.

'Terminate with extreme prejudice', anyone?

>
>Dump is an interesting one. I think its general usage in N. America is 'to defecate' or 'do
>a dump'. Entirely different meaning in UK. "Take the rubbish to the dump (landfill)", or
>"Dump the leaves from the wheelbarrow over there".

A variety of meanings to that one, as well; 'defecate' is one but there
are uses for more polite society as well.  'Rubbish tip' (as in 'the city
dump'), to drop unceremoniously ('I dumped off a load of manure at the
city dump'), to cease a particular form of relationship ('My significant
other dumped me'), a generally disorderly or unpleasant place ('What a
dump!' - Bette Davis in 'Beyond the Forest', 1949), etc.

DD
```

```
> >So :        31st May, 1999
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> doesn't sort very well, though.

That's where I learned this.   Any format that depends on knowing where the user
is from is IMHO "wrong".


> >The one which really blows my mind, when referring to stocks/shares, "The
> >market dropped by
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> precipitation'.

Either seem silly - why use "per cent" if parts per 100 aren't significant?  
When I was a child I was taught to calculate percentage by dividing the number
and multiplying by 100.   It took me a while to catch on why we multiplied by
100, as I honestly didn't see any difference between .05 and 5%, so I didn't see
that we needed a "multiply by 100" step to translate from one to the other.


> A variety of meanings to that one, as well; 'defecate' is one but there
> are uses for more polite society as well.  'Rubbish tip' (as in 'the city
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> dump!' - Bette Davis in 'Beyond the Forest', 1949), etc.

What's odd is that it seems to have become acceptable to say "poop" when we are
talking about what we have stepped in (or what my grandchildren do in their
diapers).   But "shit" is only acceptable when it is used to describe what
someone says or does.   (Am I bull-pooping you?)
```

```
Language use changes in different places and times... but I still recall
the amused grin I could not suppress when I was taking the elevator back
from lunch at One Travellers' Plaza in Hartford, Connecticut... Old Yankee
Insurance Money built that place and it still reeked of it, even after all
those years... anyhow, a fellow in his mid-to-late 20's got on at one
floor and a woman he knew of similar age got on at another... as we moved
upwards they discussed the various aspects of their childrens'
developments and one suddenly remembered that a promise had been made
earlier... and intoned 'Oh, that's *right*, I *forgot*... have *I* got a
potty-book for *you*!'

And then... have I ever mentioned the time that I returned a niece to
Respected Sister-in-Law after babysitting for an afternoon and I was given
the gift of The Look when the youngster sang out 'Mommie, I gotta cop a
squat!'?
```

```
On 5 Jun 2003 13:43:54 -0400, docdwarf@panix.com enlightened us:

>In article <lakudvklt8bo3aubof441vr75445m2m4qr@4ax.com>,
>SkippyPB  <swiegand@neo.NOSPAM.rr.com> wrote:
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
>DD

Aaah, but I trained at Ft. Campbell and Ft. Polk!!  Actually,
according to "Speaking Freely: A Guided Tour of American English from
Plymouth Rock to Silicon Valley" by Stuart Berg Flexner and Anne H.
Soukhanov (Oxford University Press, New York, 1997). the term "grunt"
was originally used by U.S. Marines to differentiate infantrymen and
aviator.  However, the word was picked up by soldiers in Viet Nam and
adopted by all infantrymen.  I heard the term in basic training and in
my tour of Viet Nam.
```

```
Good response. I have 4 years until I can retire but I would like to work
10 more years. I like learning new things but I am sick and tired of
reading, reading, reading and never actually getting to use this stuff. I
personally find actually using it to be a strong motivator for both
learning and remembering. When I do not use it I find I seem to lose it.

I am currently reading a book on components in Visual Basic, but I keep
getting bogged down. I would be reading about COBOL components and OO COBOL
but I am not because of the cost of a compiler. VB, C, C++ are all useful
in their own way but I prefer COBOL to any of them.

Your are right about it being a bet either way.  I am sure I will probably
buy your book eventually and Howard's CDs also. I already have a Mike
Murach book on VB ,NET just waiting for me. Someday I might even shell out
for a compiler if the demands of daily life ever allow me to accumulate
enough savings.
```

```
"Charles Hottel" <chottel@cpcug.org> wrote in message news:<01c32861$047326a0$a093f243@chottel>...
> Good response. I have 4 years until I can retire but I would like to work
> 10 more years. I like learning new things but I am sick and tired of
> reading, reading, reading and never actually getting to use this stuff. I
> personally find actually using it to be a strong motivator for both
> learning and remembering. When I do not use it I find I seem to lose it.

I agree. 

That, by the way, has on occassion been my excuse for programming
after hours.

One of the cool things about "PC" based programming (including the
whole .NET technology suite) is that it is much easier to apply the
tools to your personal life. While at home, any time that I'm using
the computer and come across an "opportunity" for developement, I try
to recognize it as a chance for more hands-on practice. Not that I
don't get enough practice at work... (however, sense I've entered the
world of mgmt...well, you know the story I'm sure)

For example, let's say that you are using your personal computer (at
home) and decide to backup your "MyDocuments" folder. After doing this
a few times, you come across the notion that you could write a .NET
program (language of your choice) that will present a Windows GUI Form
that allows you to select a folder location (or go with a default),
ZIP/Compress the contents, and copy to a particular location. Later,
let's say that you have refined this down to a one-click operation.
Then, you decide to automate the whole process. Still with .NET, you
create a Windows Service that runs quietly in the background - waking
up according to a schedule, ZIP/Compressing your folder and copying
your data to a preferred location. Heck, maybe you'll even add in the
SMTP type code (shown in Howard's earlier posting) to send you an
email to notify you each time...just to let you know that the
operation completed ok.

Of course, you could go out and purchase a package off the shelf (most
likely) that does these things. However, with .NET, you could easily
build your own. I agree that having a purpose, makes learning and
remembering easier. I try to remind myself that when I'm at work, my
job is often to make life easier for the business, for the user's, and
to increase the productivity of my programming staff. While at home, I
try to reward myself by remembering to make life easier for myself
(letting my computer work for me <g>) if possible.
 
Not a new concept...but with "PC" based programming, it's much easier
to bring these benefits into your own home.

Now, if I could just write a .NET program to "automate" taking out the
garbage <g>

> 
> I am currently reading a book on components in Visual Basic, but I keep
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

Many of the existing VB programmers (that are using VB version 5 or 6)
are gearing up to start learning VB.NET. They ARE in for a learning
curve. You might use caution if you are in the process of learning VB.
I mean...If you are "starting" to learn VB and not already into
VB.NET...I would think seriously about switching to VB.NET first. This
will save you the trouble of going through the learning curve twice.
Once, learning VB. Then, unlearning some of the VB5/VB6 stuff to start
learning VB.NET.

- cheers.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** richardson@eclecticsoftwaresolutions.com (Chris Richardson)
- **Date:** 2003-05-29T22:42:57-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b11feff.0305292142.5baa3f72@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <2934582.1054186897@dbforums.com> <b11feff.0305291104.5a1d8f99@posting.google.com> <bb5nkh$opm$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
Your comments:

> I am interested though in what happens once I read that book (and do the
> exercises).   Do I have any strategies available to persuade my place of
> employment to consider using CoBOL & .NET in its next project?   Are their
> employers who are looking for people with this skill set?
> 

If your place of employment includes both a mainframe platform and
Microsoft/Windows platform, chances are you will not be the first one
rallying behind a transition to .NET (notice the language neutral
gesture). It's just that you will be speaking from a different side of
the fence. While not alone, your voice will likely get noticed (it's
happened for me, at least). Why?

I've seen the eyes of some execs light up just at the thought that
finally there might be a new legacy-leveraging opportunity to explore.
Finally, (it seems) they can instantly boost the moral of the
COBOL'ers that may have complained about not getting chances to
explore the "new stuff". Finally, the business can further tap into
that wealth of business-rules knowledge that exists in the mainframe
programming department.

So, once you get this far, the reality of which language will be used
is almost a secondary point - atleast to the execs. The only point
they seem to focus in on is that "wow, my mainframe guys can do .NET
now!" - a double bonus and an easier justification for investing into
.NET in the first place (this makes the Windows/Web veterans happy
also).

Then, home at the ranch, the (.NET) developers can deal with which
language to use for which portions of the application. The .NET
platform fully supports the idea of being multi-lingual. In a large
.NET application, you could conceivably have an application made up of
several different langauges (of course, organization and
maintainability is still important).

You asked if employers are looking for "that" skillset. I'd like to
put it this way, the skillset and "packaging" that I have in mind
would suggest that mainframe developers interested in .NET become:

1st, .NET Windows/Web developers (.NET, infrastructure and all)

2nd, developers that typically already knows the "business" side
inside and out. Plus, a developer that can "read" all of the "business
rules" buried inside of the legacy mainframe COBOL code.

3rd, a knowledgeable developer that understands the importance of
Quality and design.
 
4th, a developer that happens to continue to code in COBOL AND one
that is flexible to quickly adapt and code in whatever other .NET
enabled languages that are needed (i.e. VB.NET, C#, etc.)

Put this way, yes, this skillset is in demand.

- chris.
```

#### ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

```

<gee - I don't know if the COBOL world can handle 2 "Howards"..>

The argument regarding COBOL being easier to maintain is a of course an
old stereotypical argument held in esteem by the COBOL community.
Unfortunately it holds little water when you consider the following:

1. No language is easy to maintain  if you can't find experience
   programmers to do so. And fewer and fewer COBOL programmers are
   coming into the market....

2. When newer languages abstract tedious chores to class libraries and
   offer features such as inline expressions, where it appears to me
   (and I could be wrong..) the COBOL committee decided to force users
   to use the old verbose COBOL way of doing things, then COBOL is most
   certainly not easier to maintain. It  is simply verbose. And it
   doesn't need to be.

Regarding the question as to why an Enterprise should pick COBOL . the
NET environment, there are two contributing factors:

1. They have a large COBOL system they wish to Migrate (why rewrite in
   another language if a good COBOL compileris available in the .NET
   realm, which  it is?).

2. They have  COBOL programmers they wish to involve in the task.

If neither of the above it true, then I highly doubt you will find lots
of new development going on in COBOL. Which is exactly the problem the
COBOL world has been experiencing these past 15 years or so.

What we need is a resurgence in the language and  this would have to
have grass roots support in Academia. OO COBOL is a great language to
use to learn OO concepts. Unfortunately, you can probably count on your
fingers (outside the .NET realm) the number of enterprises who have
fielded Production applications in OO COBOL. And therein lies the
vicious circle......

-Howard Hinman
```

```
In regards to Chris' comments, thanks for the agreeable comments. And
while not trying to plug anything, I am the Author of the .NET Training
CD's (also available at Amazon with all 5-star reviews from real COBOL
programmers, incidentally). And before anyone starts pointing fingers at
me for self promotion (which was not the intent of my posts here),
please note I receive no royalties or other income from sale of this
book. I did it out of the love of COBOL under a straight hourly
agreement. And this book (actually 23 hours of training videos on Cd's)
is targeted at Mainframers and PC COBOL programmers alike.

Regarding the other Howard's comments about compiling into the .NET
environment, he is correct, you can typically bring  applications from
the mainframe down into .NET using Fujitsu's .NET COBOL compiler. But I
think you get a little more than the same application. You get a very
modern and rich set of tools for COBOL development, including a
fabulous debugger. And you also have managed code (MSIL) and the world
is now open to modernize your COBOL application - make it a web
service, put a GUI front end on it, mix it easily with other languages,
etc... But more than anything, you get the application off the
mainframe and onto a far less expensive platform where things are
actually happening technology wise....

-Howard
```

```
Hello Howard,

I really respect your opinion and expertise, but I differ in what is
my own.

Howard served as the technical editor for my book Sams Teach Yourself
COBOL in 24 hours.

I want to comment on just a few of your points below.

hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com> wrote in message news:<2937647.1054243276@dbforums.com>...
> 
> Regarding the other Howard's comments about compiling into the .NET
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> actually happening technology wise....

The thing is - modernizing your application with a GUI has been
possible for a very long time.  .NET is not the first place this
became available.  Making a web service from COBOL hasn't been hard
either.  Since the Soap toolkit was first released, Fujitsu and other
compilers have been able to compete in this arena.  Finally - I take
exception to the idea that "off the mainframe" is "onto a far less
expensive platform" or the implied "things aren't happening technology
wise" on mainframes.  I disagree.  In many circumstances - in fact
most - the cost of offloading mainframe to other platforms can cause
an INCREASE in expense - and not a savings.  I can hardly keep up with
the new releases from IBM for new mainframe technology.  What may not
be happening is that companies may not be updating - because what they
have works and there is little financial pressure to update.

I agree with your comments in other postings about new programmers
choosing COBOL first - and I'm not certain what to do about it.  What
needs to happen is that they need to be educated in "The right tool
for the right job" philosophy.  There are many things where I choose a
tool other than COBOL because they are better suited to the task. 
However, I don't hesitate at all to use COBOL for most things -- when
it makes sense.
```

```
On 29-May-2003, hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com> wrote:

> But more than anything, you get the application off the
> mainframe and onto a far less expensive platform where things are
> actually happening technology wise....

That's what I don't quite get.   I understand changing technology to use the web
- which is bigger than the mainframe.  But I don't see the advantage of moving
from a big database machine to a bunch of separate PCs.

Moving .NET to the mainframe will open up possibilities.   Moving the mainframe
to PCs is something else.

It's about the data - and bigger and more powerful tools to handle the data,
security, and integration are what we need.   I can see how .NET can be quite
useful in this endeavor.   But replacing one program on the machine with the
data with a thousand programs on PCs is a hard sell.
```

```
I agree Strongly with Chris' comments about the many assumptions made in
NET manuals and books regarding the reader percieved level of
knowledge. .NET is a massive new environment. But once one finds a good
map through it, you will learn quickly. Many concepts in .NET are
concepts we had on the mainframe 20-30 years ago....
   I was a guest speaker at two Microsoft Conferences a few months back.
   Microsoft did presentations on an Introduction to .NET prior to my
   presentation. They use terminology like "XML Web Services" quite a
   bit. When i got up, I asked the audience how many people really
   understood what XML is. One person out of about 60 or so raised their
   hand. I then asked the same question about "Webservice". Again one
   person raised his hand (the same person). The following  day, we gave
   the same presentation in a different city. Again I did the same
   thing. Approximately 4 people out  of about 50 raised their hands.
   And there were only a few  COBOL programmers in each session,
   incidientally.
   So taking for granted that even a single term is well understood when
   teaching a new technology such as .NET can be a serious oversight and
   can very quickly lose your students in what they may quickly percieve
   as a vast technical abyss. It need not be.....
-Howard Hinman
```

#### ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-30T05:20:54+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
