```
In response to Thane's comments:

>The thing is - modernizing your application with a GUI has been
>possible for a very long time. .NET is not the first place this
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>an INCREASE in expense - and not a savings.

I have a great deal of respect for Thane as well. But I must very
strongly disagree with his comments above. I spent 12 years at Micro
Focus taking literally thousands of mainframe programmers off the
mainframe and onto PC's to do their development work. I worked with a
very high percentage of the Fortune 500 companies in doing do. WITHOUT
EXCEPTION, every single implementation on the PC had a return on
investment in less than one year - often within a few months.

I have spent the past 6+ years migrating applications from a variety
of platforms (mostly IBM mainframe) to the PC environment. WTHOUT
EXCEPTION the return on investment is staggering, and the cost of
Quad Xeon Servers as opposed to the mainframes they have easily
replaced is fractional - in hardware, software, support personel,
environmental, etc.
I was invited last year to speak at COMPUTERWORLD's Premiere 100
conference (where they award the top 100 IT people in the world). The
story I was asked to present  was the migration of all applications off
a 3090 class mainframe, which was literally rolled out the door into the
parking lot during a party at the conslusion of the migration. Here's a
link to the story:

http://www.computerworld.com/hardwaretopics/hardware/server/story/0,108-
01,68844,00.html

I was doing this again last week at a Unisys site. Same issues -
expensive old mainframe with old green screen applications. Replaced by
a dual processor Dell server. A batch application that used to take 45
minutes on the mainframe to process 1 million records runs in less than
2 minutes on the PC.

So I'm afraid you will NEVER convince me that a mainframe is a less
expensive platform to host any type of application on than high powered
PC servers. And if you run out of capacity, you buy another $30-60,000
PC server and cluster it. When you run out of capacity on a mainframe,
$30-$60,000 won's even buy you a memory upgrade...
Howard Hinman
```

```
However, what Thane says above is NOT the same thing as using PCs for COBOL

it is a better course, even for traditional COBOL mainframe development.

mainframe will not need to be persuaded of the advantage in having your own
dedicated processor available to give you instant gratification (even if it
IS an INTEL processor <G>). And you are right that the consequent freeing of
mainframe resource to do real work makes the whole exercise cost
justifiable.

to Client Server, NOT development.

cases, he might well be right. He is certainly right about the fact that
things are moving in the mainframe world. I have to admit, I have lost track
of it. (I haven't worked on mainframes for at least 10 years now, but in the
```

#### ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com>

```

Since someone else posted a program, please allow me to post one of my
favorites:

 IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
 PROGRAM-ID. EMAIL2.
 ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
 CONFIGURATION SECTION.
 REPOSITORY.
     CLASS SmtpMail AS "System.Web.Mail.SmtpMail".
 DATA DIVISION.
 WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
 PROCEDURE DIVISION.
     Invoke SmtpMail "Send" Using "hhinman@awebdomain.com"
                           "hhinman@hinmanconsulting.com"
                           "This is from COBOL - Test"
                           "This is a test Email from a COBOL program".

..the above programs compiles and executes - sending an email out.
That's all there is to it. It  is invoking a .NET class to do so. A one
statement COBOL program (procedurally) at that.
   And if you look carefully, you'll notice it's not even an OO COBOL
   program - it is a traditional procedural COBOL program with a couple
   of OO Extensions being used. So COBOL programmers can continue to
   code in the traditional procedural manner, yet interact with the .NET
   OO Framework - the best of both worlds....
-Howard Hinman
```

##### ↳ ↳ What is an OO Program? (was: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-30T03:38:27-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bb75a4$2ei$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <2939086.1054279233@dbforums.com>`

```
Howard's (no-OO program?) below reminds me of one of my "pet peeves" with
the 2003 COBOL Standard.  Others have STRONGLY disagreed with me - so I was
never able to progress this within J4/WG4, but I have always thought that
any COBOL programming guide, language reference, text book,  whatever  -
that was aimed at "OO" in COBOL should be divided into two sections:

 1) that for (basically) non-OO programs that "use" various classes methods,
whatever
        versus
 2) that for those DEFINING the classes and methods (and inheritance, etc)

        ***

In a way (probably/possibly NOT what he would say) this has to do with
Pete's views on "components".  It is my PERCEPTION that when/if there are
sufficient "business" class libraries (i.e. libraries to "accomplish"
business needs - not JUST "internal programming tasks" such as GUI's and
Collections), that it will be INCREDIBLY easy for "traditional COBOL
programmers" to move to *USING* OO "facilities" (as in Howard's example)
without getting "bogged down" in a paradigm shift when it isn't really
needed.

Some of my thoughts (in this area) come from a background where "DBA's" did
a lot of SQL (or previously IMS or even VSAM) work for "application
programmers" to use.

OBVIOUSLY, (just as I think that Pete recognizes that SOMEONE has to
"create" components), I fully understand that in "such" an environment
someone will need to create the classes and methods.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is an OO Program? (was: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote in message
news:bb75a4$2ei$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net...
> Howard's (no-OO program?) below reminds me of one of my "pet peeves" with

>  1) that for (basically) non-OO programs that "use" various classes
methods,
> whatever

…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pete's views on "components".

It's OK Bill, what follows is EXACTLY what I would say, you are not putting
words in my mouth...<G>

> It is my PERCEPTION that when/if there are
> sufficient "business" class libraries (i.e. libraries to "accomplish"
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I'll go further... It doesn't even matter about the Class libraries. If the
functionality is encapsulated in a component, it can be used by anyone
(whether they are OO programmers or not). I tried to cover this with
examples in the "Components 101" paper I wrote at:
http://www.aboutlegacycoding.com/default.htm?AURL=%2Farchives%2Fv5%2FV50101%2Easp

There is no REQUIREMENT to understand Classes, Inheritance, Polymorphism,
Encapsulation, or ANY aspect of OO programming, in order to use components,
provided the component is properly documented with its Events, Properties,
and Methods explained, and sample code on how you would use it in various
environments. (I go to great trouble to ensure that all components I create
do have such accompanying documentation; in some cases there is a method
inside the component that allows it to document itself.)


> Some of my thoughts (in this area) come from a background where "DBA's"
did
> a lot of SQL (or previously IMS or even VSAM) work for "application
> programmers" to use.
>
It is a good analogy.

> OBVIOUSLY, (just as I think that Pete recognizes that SOMEONE has to
> "create" components), I fully understand that in "such" an environment
> someone will need to create the classes and methods.
>

Yes, absolutely. The difference is that whereas in the past, virtually every
Corporation had its in-house IT department writing bespoke software, this is
becoming less and less viable.

The "computer programming" became an end in itself, sucking up valuable
corporate resource. The "support of the Business" which is what it is all
ostensibly about, became lost in a smokescreen of technobabble.

More and more corporations are saying: "Enough. We are not a computer
company, we are an <Insurance, Banking, Airline, whatever> business. We can
buy a computer solution for a fraction of what it is costing us to build and
maintain one".

So they implement SAP or Siebel or something similar, do a bit of BPR to
bring their existing processes in line with the package (heresy! never would
have been tolerated even 10 years ago!) and away they go, running their
<Insurance, Banking, Airline, whatever> business.

So you are right, Bill, that SOMEONE will need to write the Class libraries
and components of the future. But it WON'T be in-house IT departments, who
can buy this stuff off the shelf for a few hundred bucks and plug it into
their standard business software.

(This is the basis for my statement: "'Computer programming' was a
phenomenon of the latter half of the 20th century."
 In the 21st Century we don't need it. Users can interact with smart
software to generate applications specific to the business; there is no need
for "computer programmers" to have the requirements explained and specified,
with the subsequent communication gaps that lead to imperfect solutions...
(Part of this achievement is because the general public are becoming MUCH
more computer literate, and the software is getting much smarter. This means
the "middleman" (read "computer programmer") is becoming less and less
necesary...)

The modern IT department exists to service the Network and keep it running.
"Computer programming" is no longer part of it.

Pete.

<snipped cool COBOL program from Howard Hinman>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Rank & Titles

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>

```

On 30-May-2003, richardson@eclecticsoftwaresolutions.com (Chris Richardson)
wrote:

> Could not agree more...all of the "Computer Programmers" at my place
> of employement (a fortune 500 company) carry the actual title of
> Software Engineer. This titles seems fitting - given the more modern
> of "plug-in, component based" development.

I was an SE over a decade ago, working for EDS.   The big advantage of that
title was that it didn't mean much.    Why is this an advantage?  EDS supplied
programmers to work in other people's shops.  People looked at your title and
compared it to titles of their programmers and used that to create expectations
about what you could or couldn't do.


OT:
A while back the Colorado State Patrol gave their officers titular promotions -
so that their Captain was the same rank as other Captains in police work.    Of
course "police officer" is an odd one.   We don't see police sergeants
complaining when we call them "officer" the way we would hear military
sergeants.   I suppose this came about to make it easier to accept their
authority.   Is the history of police ranks similar to that of the military?  
Where the elite would start off as officers, and the commoners as enlisted - and
rarely would they change?    I've read that in the USSR they had Russian
officers, Lithuanian Non-Coms, and Everybody else as enlisted.   I wonder how
similar police used to be to these stereotypes.

T.E. Lawrence enlisted after the war - this was pretty much a masochistic
gesture though made by someone who was somewhat different.
```

```
"Peter E.C. Dashwood" wrote:

> So you are right, Bill, that SOMEONE will need to write the Class libraries
> and components of the future. But it WON'T be in-house IT departments, who
> can buy this stuff off the shelf for a few hundred bucks and plug it into

While it makes sense to move to packaged modules(components) - yes the SOMEONE(S) -
perhaps a zillion little people living on the borders of Nepal will need to
understand , "Classes, Inheritance, Polymorphism, Encapsulation".

Failing that you will finish up with somebody as skilled at carpentry as I am.
'What's your problem if I use a hammer to bash a mallet to put a wooden peg into a
hole ?".

There's one real problem I see with components, not related to techniques at all -
the library or cataloguing function. I can't recall whether or not it was Activex,
but for the sake of discussion let's take that 'Appointment Grid" you used for the
salon, and assume you found it as a component. "Great !", you say. Then I come along
some eighteen months later and tell you I did a google and found Component X. "Bugger
it!", you reply. "Wish Id' known that one was around when I was searching. It has
additional whistles and bells I could have used".

So that people aren't chasing their tails with 'indifferent' components, a catalogue
needs to be almost encyclopaedic, with a hierarchy itemizing what is available.
Possibly issued by something called "Component Software Society" ???

I don't know whether it is an obstacle, but I have seen some offered that appear to
address specific languages - indicating perhaps they aren't 'universal'.

Remember the famous "Invoking Up and Down" and Huib's excellent example of invoking
subordinate classes from super - "down" rather than "upwards" ? Ticking away in the
back of my mind is that this approach could be very *useful*  It may well have merit
in designing components.

Jimmy
```

```
I have never argued that the people who develop the components would NOT
need OO knowledge, Jimmy. My point was that people who USE the components
don't need it.
>
> There's one real problem I see with components, not related to techniques
at all -
> the library or cataloguing function.

Yes, a very valid point. A number of universities are now teaching Component
based design. As a result, there are a number of "Component Catalogging"
software packages in the marketplace (try a search on Google...).  I have a
fairly primitive (by comparison) Relational database that keeps track of the
components I use, whether developed by me or others, and their
documentation, Objects, Properties, Events,  Methods, and any particular
notes I think are relevant. As I currently have less than 25 components
available, this is no big deal. Corporations where they could have 100,000
components available need something more "industrial strength".

> I can't recall whether or not it was Activex,
> but for the sake of discussion let's take that 'Appointment Grid" you used
for the
> salon, and assume you found it as a component. "Great !", you say. Then I

> some eighteen months later and tell you I did a google and found Component
X. "Bugger
> it!", you reply. "Wish Id' known that one was around when I was searching.
It has
> additional whistles and bells I could have used".

I had to smile at this as it is years old and been superseded by a better
component <G>. Anyway, "for the sake of argument" as you say...

I DID do a thorough search at the time, not just with Google, but with tools
for locating components... The grid I finally used may not have been the
best one overall, but it met ALL of my requirements. That is the basis for
selection of any component; it has Methods and Properties and Events that
provide exactly what is needed... It has been superseded not because the new
one provides more or different functionality, but because the new one (from
the same developer) is simply smaller and more efficient, and the upgrade
was a few dollars. It still continues to function without problem, exactly
as it always has. Saved me three months programming by a conservative
estimate.

>
> So that people aren't chasing their tails with 'indifferent' components, a
catalogue

> Remember the famous "Invoking Up and Down" and Huib's excellent example of

Component design is certainly based on OO design. I already covered
somewhere else, in response to a request from you, how I personally go about
designing and building components. As you might expect, I employ a
pragmatic, rather than theoretical approach.<G>. It works for me.... You
might well employ a different approach, based on Huib or anybody else. As
you are not doing it, it's rather academic don't you think <G>?

Pete.
```

- **From:** "anon" <no@no.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-31T01:16:13+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<xnTBa.691$QF2.135564@news2.telusplanet.net>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <2939086.1054279233@dbforums.com> <bb75a4$2ei$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <3ed73866_6@news.athenanews.com>`

```
> (This is the basis for my statement: "'Computer programming' was a
> phenomenon of the latter half of the 20th century."
>  In the 21st Century we don't need it. Users can interact with smart
> software to generate applications specific to the business; there is no
need
> for "computer programmers" to have the requirements explained and
specified,
> with the subsequent communication gaps that lead to imperfect solutions...
> (Part of this achievement is because the general public are becoming MUCH
> more computer literate, and the software is getting much smarter. This
means
> the "middleman" (read "computer programmer") is becoming less and less
> necesary...)
>
> The modern IT department exists to service the Network and keep it
running.
> "Computer programming" is no longer part of it.
>
> Pete.

I just find it ironic that the more companies go this way (which IMO is a
current fad) the more work I get as a developer.

#1 - Smart Software doesn't exist, because Users will (forever) be the
masters of pointing out why a system doesn't work for them.  And they are
not going to tweak that software either.  That's not their job, in their
opinion.  If one feels it's their job and succeeds in that conviction then
they should take that show on the road -- because many of my clients are
trying to leverage "$1,000,000 user-friendly software systems" to no avail.
Mostly because they don't work, and the User can't get what they need
(which, incidentally, was in the original system).

#2 - Even in the ultimate RAD IDE with the most stellar class library, you
still need someone to understand what it all means. Components and class
libraries imply a framework.  The people that understand frameworks like
.net and J2EE are not Users.  They're not necessarily programmers either.
They're usually senior analysts and software engineers.  I guarantee that if
you give a new programmer the .net/J2EE framework he will come out with
something that looks very client server.  A User?  Forget it.

#3 - I don't care what they're written in, I've never seen a packaged
solution rolled-out successfully and on-time in TEN YEARS of consulting.
Are they using class libraries, components, or other "Smart Software"?  Of
course.  Will they give you the source-code?  Doubtfully.  And then if they
do, it won't be a User who's playing with it.

As far as I'm concerned, programmers may get bored with hacking solutions
for "non-development" companies that have a mish-mash of poor packaged
solutions.  But at least they will stay employed.

The smart ones will realize that it's not worth the hassle and move to the
other side -- to companies creating "Smart Software" that doesn't deliver
til several $mill down the road.

--R
```

```
> news:3ed809d5_1@corp-news.newsgroups.com...
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Maybe I haven't communicated as well as I hoped.

I'm not suggesting that the "art" of programming will become obsolete. ( I
don't believe ANY "art" form should or can become obsolete. But that
addresses a different issue...). I am saying that in-house computer
programming as we have done for the past 40 years will be rendered
redundant. (I believe it is already. This is not based on some
hallucinogenic vision, Don, it is based on what I have seen and been privy
to in the real world, in real corporations, recently.)

Here is a brief statement of my position.

1. Users and software are getting much "smarter". (The Users, not in an IQ
sense but in their degree of "computer literacy"; I think I mentioned
elsewhere, that in the last project I managed (up until April this year)
there were more computer and IT graduates in the USER area than there were
in the IT department!

These people are becoming more and more capable of doing it for themselves,
and the improvements in software and interfaces are not only facilitating
this, it is actually encouraging them to do so... (It just so happens that
component technology is playing a large part in this, and , to a large
extent, has made and is making, it possible, but that just happens to be an
area of particular interest to me.)

2. As Users become more empowered and better informed about "things
computer" they expect and demand more. They are no longer prepared to be
"fobbed off" by overloaded IT departments. If their request for a system is
deferred or costed beyond reasonability, they will certainly "have a go" at
doing it themselves, and SOME of what they are doing is pretty good stuff
<G>. (OK, some of it is rubbish, too...but we can only expect them to get
better as they make more attempts...)

Programmers don't need to be "computer psychologists". Machines are not
smart enough (yet <G>) to need that. What Programmers DO need is to realise
that the the way w have "always" done things, is becoming less and less
viable.
The Market has changed (and is still changing), the computers (hardware and
software) have advanced in quantum leaps, and the User base is no longer
ignorant about the technology.

There is becoming more demand for direct interface between Users and
computers. Software and Methodologies that can utilise this, are winning
against "computer programming" and the Waterfall.

Systems are being being "evolved" by repeated iterations towards stated
goals, rather than being "designed" then "written" then "implemented", then
repaired...

You were pretty scathing in your post about "packages"... But you are
forgetting that yours is a niche market. It may well be true that you have
the best solution in that market (at the moment) but...right now a bunch of
spotty nerds in a garage somewhere could be plugging components together to
build an equivalent solution. Maybe they are working with a particular
trucking company, offering them something virtually free in exchange for
their application knowledge...using software tools that enable them to build
and dismantle various solutions in a day or so instead of the weeks you
might expect it to take...

Even if they're not, my point is that for the majority of mainstream
businesses, awareness is starting to grow that EVERBODY needs stuff like
Accounts Receivable, Accounts Payable, Sales Analysis, Payroll and HR, Asset
Register, Warehouse and Production Control, and these things are all
available as packages without it costing an arm and a leg to develop and
maintain them in-house.  Furthermore, component technology is making it even
easier to implement the package then "tailor" it , without having to change
the basic configuration (which many have learned to their cost is disastrous
because the package is then "non-standard").

You also claimed that you are making more money as a consultant because
people are failing to implement $1,000,000 solutions. Would it be fair to
say that this "consultancy" is based around the application your business is
based on, which, as I said before, is a niche market?

I'm not doubting what you say, Don, it is just important (for the sake of
this debate) that it doesn't get spun into something it isn't. You are not
"consulting" on IT and Business in general, right? You ARE consulting in the
sense that you can provide a specific solution  (and advice and experience)
to a specific Business sector.

> Even with humans, we need people to teach. What you are really saying is
> that the computers are becoming more intelligent than the average
employee.
>

No I'm not. You won't find anything remotely approaching that statement in
anything I've written here or elsewhere.
(If you're going to put words in my mouth, at least make them sound like
words I MIGHT have said <G>).

What I'm saying is that BOTH computers and Users are getting "smarter"
(closest word I can find without a lot more effort) and this combination is
squeezing the need for an intermediate layer (programmer), as we have
traditionally been used to. (Even that is not EXACTLY what I want to convey,
but it is as close as I can manage without writing a paper on it.<G>)

Your statement that we need people to teach Humans is definitely debatable.

I watch kids using software to educate themselves and I marvel at it. I know
a particular two and a half year old who switches on the computer, starts up
his own particular programs and interacts with them to increase his literacy
and numeracy skills, while he thinks he's playing a game...  Keyboards and
mice hold no fears for this kid (nor for most of his friends). If you think
it isn't fair to debate this using kids' software, I can offer myself (far
from being a kid...) as an example.

I learned Java, HTML, JavaScript, ASP, ActiveX/OLE/COM/DCOM/COM+ and OO
technology without the assistance of a Human Teacher. I don't claim in depth
expertise in all of them (I do in some...<G>), but I certainly know enough
to do the things I want and need to with them, and enough that other people
seem quite happy to ask for and take my advice about them. It wasn't easy,
it took time and effort, but a Human tutor was certainly not indispensable.

(Having said that, I wouldn't discourage others from using Human Tutors,
neither would I necessarily NEVER use one myself. Everyone is different, and
I was trained as a teacher so I find it relatively easy to educate myself...
This is a debate on your statement "Even with Humans we need people to
teach..." I am trying to make the point that "it ain't necessarily so..."
not that "It is never so...".

Thanks for responding as yourself.

I have tried to summarize my position, communicate it better, and correct
some misconceptions you seem to have about what I'm saying. Let me know
whether I succeeded or not <G>...

Pete.
```

```
hhinman wrote:

> Since someone else posted a program, please allow me to post one of my
> favorites:
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…

Yep you've got Pete all enthused about its simplicity and elegance. The
class
SmtpMail - as you said, that's a .NET class.

Real world - Customer places order which is accepted into application. Usual
dollar extensions, warehouse picking list etc., etc... From your ordering
system you can advise what will *actually* be shipped (noting current
shortages, as appropriate), if it will be going xxx airline or xxxx ship to
Timbuktu, ETD xxxx from yyyy and ETA xxx at zzzzz, with reference to Customs
documentation, if appropriate..

Your application quotes customer's PO #, plus you allocate your own internal
Order #, (can in turn be your invoice #). Makes sense to send actual advance
delivery sheet, but could do it e-mail, somewhat like w/p, using standard
merge paragraphs, with 'fill in the blanks'.

Lots of 'business' stuff going on here; possibility of a dialog to enter
initial order information - or Pete might detail how he would handle it with
components. No deep thought on this, but my tendency would be to use OO
COBOL classes, breaking the problem down into discrete 'entities', some
support classes, and others I write myself.

Really not sure - can't see advantage of SQL on this one, think I would tend
to have those e-mail paragraphs for merging in a Relative file where I can
get at different paragraphs by ws-RelativeKey. Yet again, could be a
(Line)Sequential - as there wont be too many paragraphs to choose from.

I use Netscape - did a quick check on N/E's Type Library Assistant, but
didn't find a specific reference. No doubt if I searched more thoroughly I
could come up with an extract for an OLE with all the methods/data types
required for 'editing' and sending an e-mail.

Briefly, where would .NET give the edge. Don't bother to send *all* the
code, (I might just sell it to someone <G>). Broad outline would do.

Jimmy
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** richardson@eclecticsoftwaresolutions.com (Chris Richardson)
- **Date:** 2003-05-31T22:00:43-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b11feff.0305312100.48f1d142@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <2939086.1054279233@dbforums.com> <3ED7F1FD.2020801@Knology.net>`

```
Your question:

> Is -this- the kind of stuff in your book?  This is -really- cool...  :)

I'll answer your question with a short and long answer :)

Kinda Short Answer: The code sample that Howard provided is a great
example showing how to take advantange of the .NET class library -
while using famaliar language syntax. Yes, this is the sort of stuff
that you will find in my book.

Very Long Answer:  In my book, COBOL and VB.NET is used for the code
samples. The code samples show how to take advantage of the .NET Class
library, the Common Language Runtime, and VS.NET productivity
features. I use a lot of famaliar mainframe terminology and analogies
to drive home the point that your existing mainframe skills should be
leveraged as you transition over to the Windows/Web .NET world. My
book is basically broken into six parts.

Part 1 (Chpts 1 - 4): Includes an overview of the .NET and previews
what the entire book will cover. Also, there are two chapters (3 & 4)
that cover what I call ".NET retraining prerequisites". In these two
chapters you will find brief introductions to topics that you "would"
normally know if you were already developing in the Windows/Web
Microsoft arena. I included these two chapters so that I could later
(in subsequent chapters) refererence these various Windows/Web
infrastructure tools/utilities with a clear concious.

Part 2 (Chpts 5 - 9): This is where I dive deeply into the .NET world.
In this part of the book, you will lean about the .NET Common Language
Runtime, the .NET Framework Class Library, & Visual Studio.NET.

Part 3 (Chpts 10 - 12): In this part of the book I cover several
examples of Data Access (text files, XML, and relational data) . Here,
you will learn how to use ADO.NET classes to access a relational
database (ie. Microsoft's SQL Server RDBMS), use .NET classes to read
and write XML, and use .NET classes to read and write regular text. In
this part of the book, all code samples are done using the CONSOLE
VS.NET Project Template.

Part 4 (Chpts 13 - 17): This is the part of the book where you will
learn to create Windows and Web applications (using the Windows and
Web VS.NET project templates). There is an entire chapter discussing
State (.NET Classes) and Cache (.NET Classes) management - this topic
is critical for Web development. There is a chapter that teaches the
use of Crystal Reports (within VS.NET) for report creation. Finally,
there is an entire chapter that teaches you how to deploy your .NET
applications (both for Windows and Web). Deployment discussion
includes the use of the Windows Installer (.msi), basic IIS
configuration, and manual XCOPY and more.

Part 5 (Chpts 18 - 20): This is the part of the book that I consider
to be "advanced". Microsoft Enterprise servers and products are
brought into the picture. I discuss several aspects of Security and
Configruation. Additionally, in these chapters you will learn how to
use .NET Classes (still with COBOL and VB.NET) in applications that
leverage features like COM+/Enterprise Services, MSMQ, Multithreading,
and Asynchornous processing.

Part 6 (Appendix A - D): This part of the book is where I dealt with
two realities: One, I needed to avoid changing the entire
design/outline of the book - to not end up with more than 20 chapters
- and to not rename or reorder any already written chapters - as they
were being edited. Hence, the use of the Appendix approach. Two, I
wanted to finish the book and still have a clear conscious. In other
words, I absolutely did not want to leave any thing "uncovered".
Hence, the reason a couple of the appendixes are rather lengthy. One
of the appendix/chapters combines the three topics Debugging, Testing,
and the Windows Services VS.NET project template (formerly known as NT
Services). One of the other appendix/chapters is a primer on the two
langauges C# and J#. For this primer, I use a code sample from one of
the earlier chapters that was formerly shown using COBOL and VB.NET.

- regards.
```

```
(2) You could download the ASP.NET WEB Matrix IDE. I have to admit,
I've not used this particular IDE. But from what I've read this is
what I understand:
(2.a) It's completely FREE
(2.b) You can develope ASP.NET applications using VB.NET or C#. I'm
not sure if you can plug in the Fujitsu NetCOBOL for .NET product into
it.
(2.c) It does NOT have some of great bells and whistles that the full
blown VS.NET does have.
(2.d) You do not need IIS on your computer
(2.e) Two good websites: 
http://www.asp.net/webmatrix/default.aspx?tabindex=4&tabid=46
http://msdn.microsoft.com/theshow/episode030/

This just may be a good way to explore - to learn...until you get your
hands on the "real thing" :)
```

#### ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-30T17:57:36+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<2941363.1054317456@dbforums.com>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`

```

regarding Pete's comments:

>I read Thane's comments as referring to moving APPLICATIONS from
>mainframe to Client Server, NOT development.

..I was discussing both scenarios. Yesterday we tested a CICS to ASP
migration tool out on a non-production PC server (lowered powered
machine). Under load testing, the CICS/COBOL application which had been
converted to ASP.NET is running approximately 9 times faster than the
mainframe response time. We are getting about 30 transactions per second
with no tuning or tweaking on a small PC Server hosting the application.

The mainframe is not a good architechure for interactive applications
because of its communications and such. This is the primary reason  the
PC became so popular as a development platform for mainframe targeted
applications. It is also the reason that running production applications
makes even better sense in a PC environment.

Someone posted something about data concerns. It is not difficult or
overly expensive to create terrabyte data bases in the PC environment. I
purchased a couple of weeks ago two 120 gig hard drives for  $108 each.
While this might be a bit dangerous to host mission critical
applications on, using RAID with these drives makes more sense.
Comparing this to mainframe storage costs is quite astronomical. I
actually use SCSI Arrays for mission critical applications, but this is
just an example....

-Howard Hinman
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2003-05-30T19:08:49+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bb8a80$5je$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <2941363.1054317456@dbforums.com>`

```

On 30-May-2003, hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com> wrote:

> The mainframe is not a good architechure for interactive applications
> because of its communications and such. This is the primary reason  the
> PC became so popular as a development platform for mainframe targeted
> applications. It is also the reason that running production applications
> makes even better sense in a PC environment.

The PC became popular because it is can be owned by one user.  Everything on it
is his to do as he wants.  Someone else's schedule does not slow him down.

Unless, of course, the user wants to use data that someone else owns.   So we
network.  We query the database on the mainframe - it checks to make sure we are
authorized to use those data and provide us with what we need.    Sounds like
interactive applications anyway.

We can move the database off the mainframe and put it on a PC that acts like a
mainframe - but that doesn't help our interactive environment by decreasing the
power of our most critical component.   We can move the programs off the
mainframe and put them on PCs.   Now we have to manage these programs that are
spread out around our enterprise at the convenience of the users.

PCs are very useful for small applications.   We no longer need to do time
sharing with large organizations to get 10 minutes of computing time per day -
we can have the PC spend 10 hours computing.   They are fine when the database
is small and only has one user.   They are fine when you are doing temporary
work (compiling, testing, modeling).  They are fine when doing ad-hoc work
(spreadsheets, internet).

But when you need to access and control a lot of data, we need a machine that
can handle a lot of data securely, quickly, and reliably.   Machines are getting
cheaper and cheaper, but our demands for more data keep growing.   We want those
data secure, we want them current, and we want everybody who needs those data to
have them now.

So we don't have a question of whether to use the PC or the mainframe - the
question is how to handle the shared data needs.
```

```
While cleaning out my drafts folder, I came across this posting that I 
vehemently disagree with.  While the particular application that Howard 
is referring to may have been running abysmally on the mainframe, in 
general, given proper tuning and resource allocation, I'll bet on the 
IBM mainframe to give very good response time.  CICS is currently used 
by most large banks because it does provide good response time under 
large loads.  What size IBM system was the application running on?  I 
have read of shops that have kept mainframes for 10 - 15 years and in 
some cases they still beat the client server du jour.  If IBM would ever 
learn the meaning of small and get the idea through its head and the 
collective heads of the various Independent Software Vendors that 200 
mips in 2003 is the same as 20 mips in 1995, you might really see the 
mainframe outperform the other platforms for the dollar in the 
environments where much of the computing is done.  Sub-second response 
time is a major goal for many CICS systems.  3000 transactions a second 
may not be unusual for some of the larger systems.  If personal 
computers could actually handle the large data loads and numbers of 
transactions for a large bank, we would be seeing a lot more of what 
Howard Hinman is talking about.

I have crossposted this to the IBM mainframe listserv to see if they 
have more specific comments on this
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Mainframe not a good architecture for interactive was Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-12-12T10:55:58+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3fd8ec4a_5@news.athenanews.com>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <2941363.1054317456@dbforums.com> <3FD7D224.90906@istar.ca>`

```

"Clark F. Morris, Jr." <cfmtech@istar.ca> wrote in message
news:3FD7D224.90906@istar.ca...
> While cleaning out my drafts folder, I came across this posting that I
> vehemently disagree with.

I take it you disagree with his statement that the mainframe is not a good
architecture for interactive use? You don't disagree with his results?

> While the particular application that Howard
> is referring to may have been running abysmally on the mainframe, in
> general, given proper tuning and resource allocation, I'll bet on the
> IBM mainframe to give very good response time.

Yes, it can. BUT it is NOT a good architecture for doing so. CICS is robust
(it ought to be; it has been around now for over 30 years) but it suffers by
comparison to a distributed network system.

CICS was NEVER as good as IMS/DC, SHADOW, or TASKMASTER, even in the IBM
mainframe environment, so it is hardly surprising that it suffers in
comparison to systems that are DESIGNED to be networked.

The main problem is the pseudo-conversational mode, where a connection is
established between the processor and the terminal, via a terminal dependent
buffer that must be reloaded each time the terminal is activated. The system
has to maintain tables that tell it what terminals are on the network and
what state they're in.

PC networks don't work that way. The devices on the network are recognised
by addressable hardware cards. There are a number of protocols (not least of
which is the ubiquitous TCP/IP protocol that makes it possible for us to
have this conversation) that simply transmit packets around the network.
Network servers pass messages to each other until the required client is
located. There is no requirement for terminal dependent buffers (DFHCOMM
area or SPA) or maintenance of state (the system is stateless), and the
whole thing is much more fail safe because if parts of the network are down
or missing, packets get re-routed "automatically". (It was designed that
way, as it was developed from a military system called ARPANET that was
designed to enable the continuance of miltary command and control after
parts of the network were destroyed by nuclear attack.)

Howard claims ASP was 9 times faster than the mainframe. Presumably, this is
compared to the CICS system running the equivalent application. I can
understand you being incensed because this was obviously not a fair
comparison. Besides, we cannot take one instance, where we don't know all
the details, as being indicative of a general case.

Certainly a modern mainframe would have no trouble maintaining 30
transactions a second on its own network, and I agree with you that (most of
the time) CICS will give "fast" response (certainly within 2 seconds).

The problem is that when you come to compare the cost of a LAN, served by
servers with the equivalent performance, against the above system, the
mainframe loses badly. The Client/Server solution would be less than one
fifth of the mainframe cost (I am looking at the TOTAL mainframe cost, which
includes infrastructure support, systems programmers,  etc.)

That is probably why Client/Server has been encroaching on what was
previously the mainframe's domain.

> CICS is currently used
> by most large banks because it does provide good response time under
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> environments where much of the computing is done.

 It isn't about MIPS (although they certainly help). It is about bandwith
(which is increasing each year). And you are correct that it is about
dollars (this is where the mainframe is losing badly...)

> Sub-second response
> time is a major goal for many CICS systems.  3000 transactions a second
> may not be unusual for some of the larger systems.

It isn't for some Internet sites either. GOOGLE copes with several billion
transactions per day, without requiring manframes.

> If personal
> computers could actually handle the large data loads and numbers of
> transactions for a large bank, we would be seeing a lot more of what
> Howard Hinman is talking about.

Well, they can and we are.

The reason it is taking so long is because the transition from Legacy to New
Technology is risky and complex. Nevertheless, it IS happening, even in the
large banks you are talking about.

Banks are probably the last stronghold of the mainframe because they are not
quite as driven by cost considerations as other businesses, and they are
inherently conservative and resistant to change. I believe WestPacTrust (a
major Australasian Bank) has moved almost totally to client server over the
past 10 years. Their online and phone banking is exemplary and they seem to
get the statements right <G>.
I don't know what the transaction rate is but it would be "non-trivial".

I have to agree with Howard's statement that the mainframe architecture is
not good for interactive use, insofar as it "suffers by comparison". (It
isn't just the processor architecture; it is the network architecture also.
So far, mainframes have not coped well with this. If the manframe is to
become another powerful network server (and some organisations see it in
this role) then the price would need to drop significantly and the system
software would need to improve significantly. CICS just won't cut it...)

I see the future role of the mainframe as Batch Processing, until such time
as online systems become efficient enough to remove the need for Batch
processing altogether.

Pete.
```

- **From:** "PAUL RAULERSON" <pkraulerson@verizon.net>
- **Date:** 2003-12-13T04:22:07+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<PtwCb.972$gk1.274@nwrddc01.gnilink.net>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <2941363.1054317456@dbforums.com> <3FD7D224.90906@istar.ca> <3fd8ec4a_5@news.athenanews.com> <121220032147157211%usrr@mac.kat> <3fda3129_3@news.athenanews.com>`

```
Webserver use stateless communication?
 Maybe in the last century. We programs and
applications are almost an EXACT model of
the CICS psuedo conversational mode, with both
a server side "session" and invisible information stored
on the web page itself acting as the DFHCOMMAREA.

And in fact, a client/server LAN is often, in fact almost always
more expensive than a mainframe solution once you break the
100 user barrier. LANS, in particular LANS driven by Windows,
are complex and very prone to error.

There is also the VERY real issue that PC's cannot handle
high speed I/O to DASD or networks, nothing at all compared
to even a modest mainframe.

In our shop, we connect the PC servers to an ESS-800 shark
via 2gbs Fibre, and that fast dual processor PC will take about
3 mins to write out 1 million 2048 byte records. (about 2 gigabytes.)

Under Linux/390 in a VM guest on a z800 connected by Ficon-2
to the same shark, those same 1 million records right out in 20 seconds.
That's on a 192MIP IFL.

Using that mainframe for file & print serving on the PC LAN network
makes a LOT of sense. It sips at the MIPS and blasts the I/O around
at speeds that leave *any* PC in the dust. Despite the fact that the
PC has many *many* more MIPS available.

By the way, using CISAM, you can right 1 million single keyed records
out to the shark in 1 min and 7 seconds. The PC takes well over 11 minutes.

Read performance blazing fast too.

The cost of the mainframe and the shark, including three years of full
maintenance
and support, and the cost of z/VM too - are easily matched by the cost of a
few PC servers and the maintenance staff needed to support them.

And the mainframe really doesn't need a full time sysprog either.

-Paul
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Mainframe not a good architecture for interactive was Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** "PAUL RAULERSON" <pkraulerson@verizon.net>
- **Date:** 2003-12-14T14:36:05+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<pz_Cb.12010$gk1.2255@nwrddc01.gnilink.net>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <2941363.1054317456@dbforums.com> <3FD7D224.90906@istar.ca> <121220032147157211%usrr@mac.kat> <PtwCb.972$gk1.274@nwrddc01.gnilink.net> <qrGCb.4190$gk1.3820@nwrddc01.gnilink.net> <217e491a.0312131200.4a23194d@posting.google.com>`

```
I was on my laptop, and I have not yet figured out how to get Linux to talk
over a wireless network with the
Orinoco card installed in it. It is on the agenda to replace the Windows XP
Prof on this laptop with either Linux,
or if necessary, MacOS-X.

Seriously, I don't believe in using PC's to run the business that pays my
paycheck; but also I think they are a great thing
for individual users. The problem is really Microsoft Windows. One of the
things I got snagged on is separating crashes caused by hardware from
crashes caused by Windows. The latter is by *far* the more common issue.

At work, I use Citrix to access Microsoft applications; except for a handful
of my users with special requirements,
everyone in the building is running Linux on the PC at their desktop, and
using the Linux ICA client to connect to
Citrix. They do not realize that their 'PC' is not a Windows PC at all. This
has led to a dramatic savings in
maintenance cost over the past three years; a couple of PC's have had
hardware failures, but that is to be expected
since they are now approaching five years old. A Pentium 200 machine drives
the screen nicely under Linux and with
Cortex, the users don't complain about speed. (Word will come up almost as
fast as you can click the icon,
same for all the other applications.)

The real benefit though is that we gain some measure of centralized control
over the Windows stuff, users can NOT install programs on "their PCs" or
even install Active-X controls in their copies of I.E. That has saved us a
lot of grief. Virus
checking and control is all centralized, and so is control over who can see
what application(s).

This is what begins to expose the problems with PCs. We drive about 130
users off two Compaq DL360 machines.
Believe me, we are not even taxing the CPU doing that, and the servers have
plenty of storage in them. DASD is
provided by a network share from a single machine talking over Fibre to the
SAN. It all runs very fast and is tuned
very well. Until Windows decides to hiccup because someone put a strange
printer on the network. Or more rarely,
the PC decides to hiccup because a heat sensor has decided it is too warm,
or a power supply blows, or so on.
Or one of the DL380s, in particular the one acting as a file/print server
hiccups because the "motherboard riser"
has an electrical problem.

That is one of the reasons I am looking hard at moving the file/print
serving stuff to a Linux partition on the mainframe. I'm not sure it will
work perfectly yet, as the Windows stuff was setup to use 'Active
Directory', but heck, I intend to try. As a side benefit, the I/O capability
from the Mainframe is enormous. And as a real benefit, I can just back up
the darn
volumes either from Linux or z/VM, and be able to restore things like the
'Exchange Data Store' without a million gyrations. (Can anyone guess how
complex it is to back/restore an Exchange server? Makes backing up a 10K
user Oracle system scattered over a bunch of AIX systems look trivial! :)
Also, I don't entirely trust the idea of
Vertias (sp?) PC software being able to actually restore a windows system to
another replacement machine if
necessary. No bootable tapes, no easy volume backup and restore, many arcane
things to worry about
like "System State" recovery. (I.E. You have to install a new copy of
Windows, then restore the 'System State' over
it. Bah! I'll DDR the blasted volume of to tape or just "dd" it to tape from
Linux. One way or another... :)

Okay okay... I'm off the soapbox.

<grin>
-Paul
```

```
In article <3fda9e76_6@news.athenanews.com>, 
dashwood@enternet.co.nz says...
<snip>
> > There is also the VERY real issue that PC's cannot handle
> > high speed I/O to DASD or networks, nothing at all compared
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> See above.
<snip>

Any time you are ready to eat crow, humble pie, etc., let me 
know, I'll do my best to help you out.  ;-)

Just to give you a few ideas --

PC is BUS centric. When bus is busy, nothing else can transfer 
data. So only one device adapter can be functioning at a time 
(even with bus mastering). 30-60MB/sec bursts are what can be 
done?

Mainframes are MEMORY centric using ECC type memory. When the 
memory is being it is only being accessed by one of n ports (n 
port memory) which means that n-1 other processors can be 
accessing it!

Now, without going into bus speeds and widths, each I/O Channel 
is a processor. That means that each I/O channel can be doing 
asynchronous I/O independent of any other channel or CPU.

Now with buffering techniques, 8 channels at 20MB/sec sustained 
rate(s), how much data can a modest mainframe (old, with 
original fibre optics) handle when the channels are at 50% 
capacity? Now look at a z/800 with 4 FICON channels (current 
fibre based channels) can do -- somewhere over 120MB/sec 
sustained.

I'm just warming up. I could get the actual data. How about you 
supplying the specs on the Intel based motherboard of your 
choice (I don't care how many CPUs it has).
```

```
OK. I choose the Intel IOP321...It isn't even a Motherboard; you could place
SEVERAL of these on a Motherboard...

The Intel IOP321 I/O processor is Intel's fifth generation I/O processor. It
is the first I/O processor to integrate an Intel XScale microarchitecture
core and a PCI-X interface. Many storage, networking, and embedded
applications require fast I/O throughput for optimal performance. The IOP321
is a highly integrated, cost-effective I/O system on a chip that delivers a
two-fold performance boost over its predecessor, the Intel IOP310 I/O
processor chipset, in I/O-intensive applications.

The IOP321 is especially well suited to networked storage applications
including RAID (Redundant Array of Independent Disks) adapter cards, ROMB
(RAID on motherboard), and other storage applications. Its small package
size, high data throughput, and integrated AAU/XOR provide an optimized
solution for these applications. In addition, the IOP321 is an ideal choice
for applications requiring a high performance I/O subsystem in a
tightly-integrated environment.

The IOP321 introduces a powerful combination of technical advancements. The
133 MHz PCI-X interface achieves up to 1 Gbyte per second throughput, a
two-fold increase over 66 MHz PCI. The internal bus operates at 200 MHz and
offers internal bandwidth of up to 1.6 Gbytes/second. The IOP321 also
features a 200 MHz DDF SDRAM controller with ECC that supports up to 1 Gbyte
of 64-bit DDR SDRAM, two times that of the previous generation. It contains
a 100 MHz, 32-bit local bus that is excellent for embedded applications
requiring a connection to non-PCI peripheral components such as ASICs, flash
memory, or DSPs.

The IOP321 has additional features that accelerate I/O throughput. A
2-channel DMA controller facilitates increased PCI-to-memory throughput and
memory-to-memory throughput. The application accelerator unit contains a
hardware-based XOR capability and a 1 Kbyte queue to accelerate RAID-related
parity calculations. The application accelerator speeds transfer of read and
write data to the memory controller and computes data parity across local
memory blocks.

Product highlights
  a.. 32-bit high-performance CPU (400, 600 MHz) based upon Intel XScale
microarchitecture

  b.. Integrated 64-bit PCI-X interface (PCI 1.0A, PCI 2.2)

  c.. 200 MHz DDR SDRAM with ECC (1 GB of 64-bit memory, 32-bit mode
supported)

  d.. Intel� Superpipelined RISC technology (7-stage integer, 8-stage
memory)

  e.. 32 KB data cache, 32 KB instruction cache

  f.. 2 KB mini-data cache

  g.. ARM Version 5TE compliant

  h.. 32-bit local bus (100 MHz) / Flash I/F

  i.. 1.6 GB/s internal bus (200 MHz)

  j.. 2 DMA channels

  k.. 2 Serial (I2C) + SPI Port

  l.. Application Accelerator Unit with hardware-based XOR capability and 1
Kbyte queue

  m.. Watchdog timer, 2 programmable timers (Auto-reload, programmed
duration, selectable prescaling)

  n.. 1024-byte DMA and 4096-byte ATU buffers

  o.. 8 general-purpose I/O pins, 4 SDRAM output clocks, and integrated
timers

  p.. Performance Monitoring Unit

  q.. 544L PBGA (35mm)
(BTW, I don't intend to pursue this argument and I have no intention of
getting into a pissing contest on fastest I/O.

I have responded because you asked for me to support my argument and it is
obvious to me that your knowledge of technology advancement is sadly lacking
in some areas. (Areas where your bias and preconceived ideas apparently
forbid you to look...)).

I am not "anti-mainframe" (made a living off them for over 20 years) and I
really don't care whether people think they are better suited to networks,
and PCs are just toy boxes with no "real" computer power, or not.

My post (if you had read all of it) was fair and balanced. My main criticism
was levelled at CICS rather than mainframe hardware.

BOTTOM LINE:  Mainframes are currently NOT a good architecture for
interactive networked applications. (Insofar as they suffer by comparison to
systems that ARE a good architecture for interactive networked
applications.)

Neither in Hardware terms, nor in Software design. End of Story.
```

```
In article <3fe2c0e8_9@news.athenanews.com>, 
dashwood@enternet.co.nz says...
> 
<snip>
> Well, I'm not hungry at the moment, thank you...
<snip>

I think you will be...
<snip>

FICON: each over 120MB/second sustained. FICON Express, 
somewhere around 200MB/second sustained. Note, that's not the 
burst rate, that is sustained data transfer(s) rate. That's over 
2Gb/second, and that is PER FICON channel.

MUX Channel: Parallel channel support has ended.

Bus width and speeds. Well, since I signed an NDA with IBM, I 
guess I can only say that:

CPU to MEMORY ports are 256 BYTES, not bits, ECC, somewhere 
above 800MHz clock.

Assume a system with a 3 GHz clock with a 32 bit bus and memory 
speed to match: would be approximately equal to 
6,597,069,766,656 bytes/sec (no error detect/ correct). WOW! 
that would be a mainframe burner if only this were possible 
today.

Notice that a 256 byte bus running with a 900MHz clock, that's 
9,895,604,649,984 bytes a second, today on a mainframe.

Again, the mainframe architecture is multi-port memory centric, 
as opposed to BUS centric. 

Last I knew, DMA meant Direct Memory Access, which does not 
negate using a BUS. 

Again, when the BUS is busy, all other devices, cards, 
processors must wait (OK, unless they have their own 
cache/buffer they can work from/with). 

Meanwhile, again, because of NDAs with IBM, I think I can tell 
you that one of the internal slower speed busses on the z/800 
with a G6 chipset has a place for you to put PCI type cards. I 
think the service processor communicates to/with those.

Now to answer someone else's posting :
"All this on a motherboard meant for a dinky-a** little dual-
processor PC server.  No it doesn't compare in throughput, 
reliability, serviceability and whatnot to a z990, but it's not 
the ludicrous Pentium/133 desktop strawman you threw up."

Well, sorry, I didn't mean to imply a "Pentium/133 desktop 
strawman". Actually, I was thinking about the 50+ Intel Servers, 
6+ Regatas (RS6000 relatives), and a few other "open system 
platforms" that a certain client of ours attempted to use to 
replace a mainframe (z/800 with 2 CPUS, 8GB "RAM", 4 FICON 
channels, several ESCON channels, a few 3745s, 1 SHARK, 2 GIGe 
adapters, etc.). BTW - they still haven't been able to get 15 
users on the NON-Mainframe systems to operate as fast as 1000 
users (CICS based applications) sharing the mainframe with 
several other applications.

It seems to me that the chipsets and motherboards that have been 
discussed are chasing what IBM did 2 generations ago on the 
mainframe systems.

Uh, how soon will we see the copper/silicon chipsets come out 
from Intel and the others?

And when will the 64bit systems from Intel, AMD and the others 
be able to actually handle, with no propagation delays, 128GB of 
RAM (C-Store to you mainframe types), wherein the ECC is done at 
the card level, with backups, so that the system phones home? 

[ECC - does that imply single bit error detection and 
correction, with double bit detection?]

Back to the Clock speed argument for MIPS or GIPS of the non-
z/ARCHITECTURE machines v. the z/ARCHITECTURE machines: There is 
no apples to apples comparison here, nor is it really possible 
to normalize between these systems. Example: when a single move 
instruction can move 256 bytes from one memory location to 
another with 1 micro-code clock cycle, how do you compare that 
to a system that has less than a 256 byte bus?

Back when I did Macrocode at AMDAHL, we were using 7 stage pipes 
in the processors. I honestly don't know what IBM is using 
today. I do know that they are up to 256 byte cache lines 
(something that some of my cohorts at AMDAHL figured was going 
to cause headaches, and sure enough, IBM ran into them). I also 
know that they are using an I-BANK and D-BANK in the cache 
(something UNIVAC use to do in C-STORE). REAL 64 bit registers 
with ABSOLUTE 64 bit addressing capability are available now 
with the G6 chipset. At this point, with CMOS, IBM has the MP 
overhead on a dual processor at about 2%. I believe the MP 
overhead for 8 CPUs is much less than the AMDAHL 5990 had 
(ECL/TTL based, ~35% overhead, 4 CPUs were ~105MIPS using 36 MIP 
CPUs).

BTW - did I mention that I'd been involved in NON IBM chipsets 
to compete with IBM? That is, to compete with IBM's mainframes? 
Or that I'd been involved in massively parallel computational 
systems that are NOT mainframe based? These suckers could drive 
a CRAY to its knees!

It is interesting that CICS is capable of large transaction 
rates (complex transactions) involving HTML and JAVA. However, 
since I'm not greatly experienced with this area, I won't make 
any claims here.

Lastly, I happen to not be a mainframe bigot (even though it 
might sound like it). Our offices are getting along just fine 
with a file server running NT 4.0 on an AMD 550MHz (running at 
500MHz) with a TEKRAM SCSI bus mastering adapter with ALL SCSI 
devices and then the work stations running various speeds of 
Intel CPUs with either LindowsOS or NT 4.0 workstation (the 
exception being this laptop -- running W/2K). ALL mainframe 
development is done using an emulation product!

Well, I guess I should quit now. I have a bit of reading to do 
in prep for some exams.
```

```
In article <20031221074759.443$pb@news.newsreader.com>, 
judmc@sunvaley0.com says...
<snip>
> This is in agreement with what I have seen with my clients. And none
> of these comments get to one of the real issues, IMO: reliability. The
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

Actually, my biggest complaint is in scalability [I will use NT 
for examples here as the problem is well known]. As you start to 
scale an NT based system, it begins to slow down because of one 
of two things: network collisions, or the I/O needs within a 
server. So then you have to add another NT server. Then you have 
to fix the network to use bidirectional switches and move up to 
faster "back bones". Then you need to add another person to the 
support team. Add another 50 (or so) users and now you need 
another NT server, another router, and another support person.

Once you get the NT network to handle 2500 people with the same 
response they got from the mainframe (for accounting, inventory, 
payroll, etc.), then we are ready to talk about RAS -- Micro
$**t's nonexistent Reliability, Availability, Servicability 
(compared to MVS, VSE, TPF, VM, OS/400, LINUX, etc.).

I'm going back to my cave now -- time to hibernate for the rest 
of the winter.
```

```
Steve Thompson <steve_nospam_t@ix.netcom.com> wrote in message news:<MPG.1a4ee4c11a70e86e9896a5@News.CIS.DFN.DE>...
> Bus width and speeds. Well, since I signed an NDA with IBM, I 
> guess I can only say that:


(Robert falls to floor stunned and speechless).


> CPU to MEMORY ports are 256 BYTES, not bits, ECC, somewhere 
> above 800MHz clock.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> 9,895,604,649,984 bytes a second, today on a mainframe.


First of all, showing 12 significant digits is just silly.  Second,
neither number is remotely accurate (hint: 3GHz*32bits=12GB/s,
900MHz*256bytes=230GB/s).

All current x86 systems use 64 bit busses to main memory.  The Pentium
4 uses 256 bit busses between L1 and L2 cache and L1D and the CPU
(since the P4 uses a trace cache and not a traditional I-cache, it's
hard to provide a single meaningful number on the L1I to CPU
interface).

The L2 to L1 cache interface on G7s transfers units of 256 bytes (but
I'm not sure what the actual bus width is, it's pretty likely that the
256 byte lines are transferred in several cycles).  The main memory
interface on G7s is 2x128 *bits*.  These end up at four MSCs, where
there's a net eight 128-bit memory ports on a 20 PU MCM.


> Again, the mainframe architecture is multi-port memory centric, 
> as opposed to BUS centric. 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> processors must wait


And on a pure switch based backplane, if one device is hitting a
particular memory bank, everyone else has to wait too.

On PCI, a device can hold the bus for no more than 256 (+/- a few)
cycles, and that's *only* when it's transferring a sequential burst,
*and* if the system configured the adapter to allow such long bursts. 
So on a 33MHz PCI bus, other devices would get a crack at the bus in
about 8 microseconds (or a small multiple of that if you had several
busmasters running 256 cycle bursts all on the same bus).

As to switched backplanes, I'm sure you'll like AMD's Opteron, which
does away with the shared memory bus.  And a number of x86 systems
larger than four processors use a switched backplane between 2 or 4
processor nodes.


> (OK, unless they have their own 
> cache/buffer they can work from/with). 


Since you're comparing to ESCON and FICON channels, I'd assume were
talking about SCSI or FC HBAs.  Perhaps you could identify a current
(non-low-end) one that *isn't* buffered?


> Meanwhile, again, because of NDAs with IBM, I think I can tell 
> you that one of the internal slower speed busses on the z/800 
> with a G6 chipset has a place for you to put PCI type cards. I 
> think the service processor communicates to/with those.
 
> Now to answer someone else's posting :
> "All this on a motherboard meant for a dinky-a** little dual-
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> strawman".


Yet that's the system you described.


> Actually, I was thinking about the 50+ Intel Servers, 
> 6+ Regatas (RS6000 relatives), and a few other "open system 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> several other applications.


Oh goody.  Another downsizing disaster.  Which has little or nothing
to do with zArch hardware design and implementation.  Let me tell you
about the shop I did some work for that (a number of years ago)
decided to rewrite their heavy VSAM based system to use (big vendor
name omitted) relational database, and suddenly their nightly batch
runs (literally) took over a week.  Perhaps ESA/390 was inherently a
bad architecture for relational databases?  Or perhaps the conversion
was botched, and 18 months latter, after a lot of rework, the system
performed reasonably well on the relational DBMS (albeit with a bit of
a CPU/memory upgrade)?

You can make a hash of things with pretty much any hardware.  Given
the horsepower you described above, I'll lean towards "botched
conversion" until somebody proves otherwise.


> It seems to me that the chipsets and motherboards that have been 
> discussed are chasing what IBM did 2 generations ago on the 
> mainframe systems.


Great.  I describe a low-end server chipset (which happens to have all
the features you said PC servers *don't*), and you complain that it's
not up to z990 standards.  Try looking at some of the big MP boxes
from IBM, Unisys, HP, etc.  They've rather more of the high-end RAS
stuff, they're also rather pricier.  And yes, the z990 stuff is hard
to beat on RAS terms, unless you look at something like the HP/Tandem
stuff.


> Uh, how soon will we see the copper/silicon chipsets come out 
> from Intel and the others?


Well, AMD has been shipping copper for some time, Intel is planning it
for the 90nm process node (which should be as shipping as Prescott in
early February).  It's also rather irrelevant.  Sure, AMD (and IBM)
probably pick up 10-20% performance, but that's hardly a huge
difference.

IBM and Intel's process technologies are generally regarded as the two
best in the world.  It's reasonable to argue that IBM has a bit of a
lead in some of the lower volume technologies (where manufacturing
cost per die is less relevant).  Intel is far and away the world
leader in the volume area (Intel is the one making a 3.2GHz processor
on bulk silicon, after all).


> And when will the 64bit systems from Intel, AMD and the others 
> be able to actually handle, with no propagation delays, 128GB of 
> RAM (C-Store to you mainframe types), wherein the ECC is done at 
> the card level, with backups, so that the system phones home? 


Well, since no one, including IBM, is shipping systems with "no
propagation delays," I'll score that a tie.  A number of IPF (aka
Itanium) systems are available which support more than 128GB.  The HP
Superdomes, for example, support at least 1024GB (don't know anyone
who's tried to order a bigger one, and I'm too lazy to go spelunking
around HPs web site to see what the actual limit is).

Most x86 servers are limited to 64GB (and most aren't 64 bits either).
 AMD's Opterons pass both tests, Intel's upcoming Prescott will
support 40 bit physical addresses, but is still 32 bit unless Intel
has a big surprise planned.  All the RAM in typical x86 servers is
fully ECC protected, and the busses (eg. PCI) at least have parity
checking and retry.  Pretty much all high end x86 servers have
provisions for a support processor which can "phone home" if they
system dies.  Most are capable of surviving the death of a processor
(although not to the same extent as a z900), and various amounts of
RAM.  The OS's in question are a different story.


> [ECC - does that imply single bit error detection and 
> correction, with double bit detection?]
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> to a system that has less than a 256 byte bus?


*sigh*

Write a program.  Compile and run it on both systems.  Use a stopwatch
(or other time measuring tool) to see how long they run.  The one with
the shorter time is the faster system for that workload.  Hint:
there's a darn good reason IBM doesn't publish SPEC numbers for
zSeries machines.  Big z9xxs score well in the I/O and reliability
departments, *not* in CPU performance.

Oh, yes, I never mentioned MIPS.

 
> Back when I did Macrocode at AMDAHL, we were using 7 stage pipes 
> in the processors. I honestly don't know what IBM is using 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> (something UNIVAC use to do in C-STORE).


Uh-huh, every x86 starting with the original Pentium has separate I
and D caches.


> REAL 64 bit registers 
> with ABSOLUTE 64 bit addressing capability are available now 
> with the G6 chipset.


Alpha, MIPS, PPC (aka POWER), PA-RISC and SPARC since well before
zArch hit the street.  Also available now are Intel's IPF (Itanium)
and AMD's AMD64.  In several of those cases some of the high address
bits are used for other purposes (for example, in IPF the address
space is divided into eight 61-bit regions instead of a single flat 64
bit area) ï¿½ not really relevant.


> At this point, with CMOS, IBM has the MP 
> overhead on a dual processor at about 2%. I believe the MP 
> overhead for 8 CPUs is much less than the AMDAHL 5990 had 
> (ECL/TTL based, ~35% overhead, 4 CPUs were ~105MIPS using 36 MIP 
> CPUs).


Scaling numbers are always funny things, and always very workload
dependent.  Yes, zSeries is pretty good, but the competition would
certainly show better scaling scores if they kept their current memory
and I/O subsystems and quartered the clock rates on the individual
CPUs.  Also much of the scaling performance is dependent on software. 
Here MVS (alright, zOS), and some of its subsystems, do pretty well.


> BTW - did I mention that I'd been involved in NON IBM chipsets 
> to compete with IBM? That is, to compete with IBM's mainframes? 
> Or that I'd been involved in massively parallel computational 
> systems that are NOT mainframe based? These suckers could drive 
> a CRAY to its knees!


(Again, Robert falls to floor stunned and speechless).  Look fella,
I've got all sorts of impressive crap in my resume too.  Claiming that
your arguments are valid because you're just so darn good isn't going
to cut it.  Appeals to authority are mostly just annoying.  Appeals to
your own authority doubly so.


> It is interesting that CICS is capable of large transaction 
> rates (complex transactions) involving HTML and JAVA. However, 
> since I'm not greatly experienced with this area, I won't make 
> any claims here.


Yes, it can, and for the right jobs it makes considerable sense to do
that.  For other workloads it can be a darn expensive place to run an
app.
```

- **From:** robertwessel2@yahoo.com (Robert Wessel)
- **Date:** 2003-12-19T13:58:00-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bea2590e.0312191358.1299d830@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <2941363.1054317456@dbforums.com> <3FD7D224.90906@istar.ca> <3fd8ec4a_5@news.athenanews.com> <121220032147157211%usrr@mac.kat> <3fda3129_3@news.athenanews.com> <PtwCb.972$gk1.274@nwrddc01.gnilink.net> <3fda9e76_6@news.athenanews.com> <MPG.1a4c36ca475b07759896a4@News.CIS.DFN.DE>`

```
Steve Thompson <steve_nospam_t@ix.netcom.com> wrote in message news:<MPG.1a4c36ca475b07759896a4@News.CIS.DFN.DE>...
> In article <3fda9e76_6@news.athenanews.com>, 
> dashwood@enternet.co.nz says...
…[37 more quoted lines elided]…
> choice (I don't care how many CPUs it has).


Mainframes certainly have impressive I/O architectures (and other
architectural features), but comparing them to the architecture of a
decade old desktop system is just silly.

Consider, for example, the fairly ordinary Intel SE7501HG2
motherboard:

- Dual Xeon (up to 3.06GHz).  Each of which will toast two or three
z990 engines in a horsepower contest.
- 12GB of ECC memory (and all "PC" servers except the very lowest-end
use ECC).
- With the right type of RAM, it's can handle full chip memory
failures (which is beyond what ECC typically can do)
- Dual channel memory, with a total of over 4GB/s bandwidth.
- Triple PCI bus, with three PCI-X slots (1 x 133MHz and 2 x 100MHz,
all 64 bit), and three slower slots.
- Built in Ultra-320 SCSI (dual) ï¿½ comparable to FICON throughput.
- Built in Gigabit Ethernet (dual, again).
- The built in I/O (as a group) and the three PCI busses do not share
any bandwidth, except ultimate access to memory (which is shared with
the CPUs as well, of course).
- The built in SCSI and GigE adapters are fully DMA capable, with full
support for independent operation, scatter/gather, and all the
goodies.  In practical terms, fairly comparable to an I/O channel
processor.
- And server class PCI SCSI and network adapters are similar, of
course (in fact, the four built-in adapters are just ordinary adapter
hardware soldered onto the motherboard with a private PCI bus).
- Yes, it has some IDE, USB, and other PC style device ports which
have typical low PC performance, but that's hardly relevant, you're
hardly forced to use them.
- And if you can't find the busses and shared bandwidth on a Z9xx, you
just aren't looking hard enough.  Busses are not, in and of
themselves, bad things, except so far as they represent a shared
resource.  PCI isn't like a block-mux channel where a slow device
still ties up the entire channel for the duration of the transfer -
many DMA transfers can be interleaved on a single bus.
- Oh, yes, FICON: there's certainly nothing stopping you from tossing
two or three Fiber Channel HBAs into one of these boxes, again, all of
which have pretty significant levels of intelligence on the adapter. 
(FC and FICON being identical, of course, except for the top protocol
layer).

All this on a motherboard meant for a dinky-a** little dual-processor
PC server.  No it doesn't compare in throughput, reliability,
serviceability and whatnot to a z990, but it's not the ludicrous
Pentium/133 desktop strawman you threw up.
```

```
"Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in message

> The problem is that when you come to compare the cost of a LAN, served by

Aren't we confusing mainframe (a machine) with client server (methodology).

Linux partitions each running as a server in a client-server model.

The mainframe of now is not the mainframe of old.  In fact I think the term
mainframe is even used to describe machines that really are mid range
servers functioning as supercharged PCs - you know the 8 Intel Xeon
processor units.  Not cheap - but also not woefully expensive.
```

#### ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-30T19:20:40+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<2941609.1054322440@dbforums.com>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`

```

Jimmy, regarding your question about the ".NET advantage"... I am
certainly not advocating that .NETis the magic cure all for COBOL
development. You are still going to write COBOL code to do lots of
things (just as others have to write VB or C# or C++ code). One of the
big rules I've followed over my career is Rule #1 of IS - If it ain't
broke, don't fix it.

NET brings into play numerous capabilities that were not easily done
with COBOL before. In your example, email is just one example of adding
some new functionality to an old application - and it can be done quite
easily. Now, I would recommend a bit more code, like an exception class
to catch any errors thrown if the email has a problem getting out -
again a few lines of code using a .NET class.

But the idea here is that I can bring my existing COBOL into this
environment and now I have all of these world-class tools to
enhance/moderize my application - IF THERE IS VALUE IN DOING SO.

The most frustating experiences in my career are going into shops who
have taken very tight and well-working COBOL applications and tried to
rewrite them into JAVA or VB and have achieved very slow and/or volatile
applications and have decided to throw them out and go back to COBOL. A
couple of weeks ago, I was in a small shop that has an accounting
package orginally written in COBOL on VAX/VMS. Great group of people and
very intelligent. They had a requirement to field a PC compatible
version So 4 years ago they began coding up a VB equivalent from
scratch. Today, it is in production, but they have lots of problems with
it - performance, rounding errors, stability, etc. And they have now
decided to take their original COBOL off the VAX and bring it into .NET
using Fujitsu. And they are going to rearchitech it to use SQL Server
instead of COBOL data files and to have a GUI available through the
Internet. And I believe they will have this done in less than a year.

Now, 4 years ago, maybe they could have fielded a system in GUI in
Fujitsu's PowerCOBOL or (God Forbid..) Micro Focus' Dialog System. But
it would not run on the internet in a Browser, and they would have to
embark on a separate source code stream to create such. Now they can do
both in one environment.

And the other compelling reason to use .NET is that even if they later
decide to stop using COBOL (heaven forbid!), they will have already
learned a new age environment (.NET), many of it's classes, it's screen
painter, it's editor, it's debugger, ect. and it will be a lot easier
for them to move onto another language. And this is a fact that has not
existed in the COBOL realm in the past - everything was proprietary to a
specific COBOL vendor. So now it makes sense to invest in training your
COBOL programmers in new technology....

-Howard Hinmna
```

```
That's always been one of my concerns with the whole client/server
methodology.  It's great for interactive processes (a la CICS) but the batch
processes, the long running tasks that are the perogative of the third
shift, it's hard for me to see the appropriateness of that.

Perhaps experience will provide insight.  I've just moved from a COBOL team
to a WEB team and it's proving to be most exciting and informative.  We'll
see.

Paul
```

#### ↳ Re: What is the future of COBOL? Answer: Irrelevant???

- **From:** hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-30T21:33:23+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<2942016.1054330403@dbforums.com>`
- **References:** `<bam318$clv$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`

```

Jimmy, I have a great deal of history with Dialog System from its very
inception while at Micro Focus. It would be inappropriate for me to
comment on anything not in the public domain, however. .NET has full
featured and far superior support for GUI applications that never touch
the Internet. It's called Windows Forms. COBOL works very well with this
technology. And you are using  the native .NET controls/classes from
COBOL, just as VB and C# do. You are not using some highly
multi-abstracted proprietary technology that is slow and kludgy and
oftentimes looks nothing like the native GUI on Windows.

Dialog System used to built upon Micro Focus' Panels/2 API which was
in turn built on top of the Micro Focus runtime. Customers could use
Panels/2 for a slighltly lower level interface into GUI, but this API
was typically quite difficult to use and was of course very
proprietary as well.

My biggest complaint about Dialog System when I was at Micro Focus was
that it did not use COBOL as the native "scripting" language to
communicate with the GUI controls and runtime elements. So you have this
proprietary unfamiliar scripting language to contend with.

I haven't used or seen Dialog System for about 7 years or so, so it is
indeed possible that Micro Focus has done some wonderous things with it
sense, and I don't intend to demean Micro Focus. There are still a few
people left there that I respect greatly.

Fujitsu, in it's PowerCOBOL product, uses very common sensical COBOL
syntax to deal with the GUI components and is far more intuitive to use
in my opinion. If you want to do GUI development on Windows and not
touch the .NET environment, you should simply use PowerCOBOL, which is
very much like Visual Basic for COBOL. But you are still creating a
proprietary solution tied closely to a vendor as you do with Dialog
System. .NET provides a single screen painter and common set of controls
for all .NET languages (although it is a significant feat to implement
your compiler to such). Fujitsu works great with it. I've been told that
Micro Focus has stated publically they are not going to bother trying to
implement to the .NET WinForms painter, but I believe they will change
their mind (if they haven't done so already), just as they later changed
their mind about refusing to do a .NET compiler.

-Howard Hinman
```

```
------------------------------------
By the way, there are a couple of alternatives to the "licenses" issue

(1) You could go with the free 60 day trial that both Fujitsu and
Microsoft offer. Visit there websites for more info.
<or>
(2) You could download the ASP.NET WEB Matrix IDE. I have to admit,
I've not used this particular IDE. But from what I've read this is
what I understand:
(2.a) It's completely FREE
(2.b) You can develope ASP.NET applications using VB.NET or C#. I'm
not sure if you can plug in the Fujitsu NetCOBOL for .NET product into
it.
(2.c) It does NOT have some of great bells and whistles that the full
blown VS.NET does have.
(2.d) You do not need IIS on your computer
(2.e) Two good websites: 
http://www.asp.net/webmatrix/default.aspx?tabindex=4&tabid=46
http://msdn.microsoft.com/theshow/episode030/

This just may be a good way to explore - to learn...until you get your
hands on the "real thing" :)

</Cut and Pasted from one of my earlier post>
```

```
Maybe, Fujitsu could make sure that the Free Microsoft ASP.NET
webmatrix IDE will handle a COBOL plug-in (instead of just VB.NET and

the free (less frills) Microsoft IDE (today, learning just VB.NET and
C#) that can also have COBOL as a language to choose. Later, when they
decide to buy the full blown VS.NET IDE, they may also choose to buy
the full COBOL plug-in.
```
